The Relative Nature of Kulli and Juz'i
Author explains the relative naming of kulli and juz'i with examples and discusses a rival etymology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a term like 'man' is called universal relative to 'Zayd' and particular relative to 'animal', and identify the role of individuators in making a universal concrete.

Core Idea
Universal and particular are relative: a term is kulli relative to what it has as parts, and juz'i relative to what it is a part of.

In logic, the terms 'kulli' (universal) and 'juz'i' (particular) are not fixed labels but depend on perspective. A single term can be universal in one relation and particular in another. This lesson unpacks the passage's explanation of this relativity and the alternative derivation that scholars debated. Understanding this helps you grasp how definitions and hierarchies work in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the relativity of the terms
The passage says 'man' is called kulli relative to Zayd, and Zayd is called juz'i relative to man. Notice that the same term can be both kulli and juz'i depending on what it is compared to.
2
Identify why 'man' is a part of Zayd
Zayd includes humanity (common essence) plus individuating attributes (mushakhkhasat) like height, color, etc. 'Man' is the common part; the individuators make Zayd a particular individual.
Individuators are necessary for external existence of a universal.
3
Examine the alternative derivation
Some derive 'kulli' from 'kull' (all) meaning encompassing individuals, and 'juz'i' from 'juz'' (part) as individual is part of universal's extension. The critic objects that in 'Zayd is a man', there is no sense of 'all' encompassing Zayd, so the derivation is forced.
This objection highlights the nuance in logical terminology.
4
Apply the concept to another example
Consider 'animal' and 'man'. 'Animal' is kulli relative to 'man' because it is a part of man (man = animal + rational). So the pattern holds at every level of the hierarchy.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مباحث الألفاظ كـ «الإِنْسَانِ مَثَلاً جُزْءٌ مِنَ المَعْنَى الجُزْئِي كَـ زَيْدِ»؛ لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى الإِنْسَانِيَّةِ وَالمُشَخَصَاتِ، وَالمَعْنَى الجُزْئِيُّ كُلِّ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَيْهِ ، فَنَسَبُوا كُلَّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا إِلَى الآخَرِ . قوله : (كـ) «الإِنْسَانِ مَثَلاً) أي : وكذا الحَيَوان» فإنَّه جزءٌ مِنَ الإنسان»؛ لتركب «الإنسان» مِنَ : الحيوانية والناطقيَّة، فسمّي «الإنسان»: «كليًّا»؛ لانتسابه إلى الكلِّ الَّذي هو زيد ونحوه، وكذا سمِّي الحَيَوَان»: «كلّيًّا»؛ لانتسابه إلى الكلِّ الَّذي هو الإنسان، وسمي زيد»: «جزئيا؛ لأنَّه منسوب إلى جزئه الذي هو الإنسان أو الحيوان ونحو ذلك. قوله : (لِاشْتِمَالِهِ عَلَى الإِنْسَانِيَّةِ وَالمُشَخَصَاتِ) المشخّصات هي: «الأوصاف التي بها أمك أن تُوجَد هويَّتُهُ في الخارج من : طولٍ أو قصر أو غلظ أو رقّة، أو بياض أو سواد، . . ونحوها»؛ لأنَّ الكلّيَّ لا يمكن وجوده في الخارج عارياً عن هذه الأوصاف. قوله : (فَنَسَبُوا ... إلخ) أي : نسبوا الكل كـ «زيد» إلى جزئه وهو «الإنسان»، فقالوا فيه : جزئي، ونسبوا الجزء الذي هو «الإنسان» إلى كله وهو «زيد» فقالوا فيه : كلّي، فمصدوق الكلي جزء ومصدوق الجزئي كلٌّ . وبحث في هذا التوجيه بعضهم بأنَّ فيه تعسُّفاً، واختار أنَّ الكلّي منسوب للفظ «كل» الَّتي للشمول ؛ لشموله لأفراده، والجزئي منسوب للجزء لكونه جزءاً من أفراد الكلّيّ. اهـ. واعترضه الشارح في حواشيه على «المختصر »: بعدم وجود الشمول في نحو: «زيد إنسان»، فتأمله
Chapter on Investigations of Words. Like 'man', for example, is a part of the particular meaning like 'Zayd'; because it includes humanity and individuators, and the particular meaning is the whole relative to it. So they related each of them to the other. His statement: (Like) 'man', for example means: and likewise 'animal', for it is a part of 'man'; because 'man' is composed of animality and rationality. So 'man' is called 'kulli' because of its relation to the whole which is Zayd and similar, and likewise 'animal' is called 'kulli' because of its relation to the whole which is man, and 'Zayd' is called 'juz'i' because it is related to its part which is man or animal etc. His statement: (Because it includes humanity and individuators) The individuators are the attributes by which it is possible to find its identity in the external, such as tallness, shortness, thickness, thinness, whiteness, blackness, and the like; because the universal cannot exist externally stripped of these attributes. His statement: (So they related ... etc.) Means: They related the whole like 'Zayd' to its part which is 'man', and called it particular; and they related the part which is 'man' to its whole which is 'Zayd', and called it universal. So the referent of the universal is a part and the referent of the particular is a whole. Some investigated this interpretation as being forced, and chose that 'kulli' is derived from the word 'kull' which indicates comprehensiveness, because it encompasses its individuals, and 'juz'i' is derived from 'juz'' (part) because it is a part of the individuals of the universal. End. And the commentator objected to this in his glosses on 'al-Mukhtasar' due to the lack of comprehensiveness in expressions like 'Zayd is a man'; so reflect on it.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage explains why the terms 'kulli' (universal) and 'juz'i' (particular) are named as such. The matn gives the example: 'man' is a part of the particular meaning like 'Zayd', because 'man' is included in Zayd along with individuating attributes. Conversely, the particular is the whole relative to the universal. The commentator explains that 'man' is called kulli relative to Zayd, and Zayd is called juz'i relative to man. He defines 'mushakhkhasat' (individuators) as the accidental attributes that make a universal concrete in external existence. Then he reports a rival interpretation: kulli comes from 'kull' (all) because it encompasses individuals, and juz'i comes from 'juz'' (part) because it is a part of the universal's extension. A critic objects that in a sentence like 'Zayd is a man', there is no sense of 'kull' (all) encompassing Zayd, so that derivation is flawed. The comment ends with 'ta'ammal' (reflect), implying the reader should ponder.

Key Terms
كلّي (kullī): Universal; a term that applies to multiple individuals. Here, it is explained as being relative; 'man' is kulli relative to 'Zayd'. [Note: Stable across logic; the discussion here focuses on the etymology of the term, not its logical definition.]
جزئي (juzʼī): Particular; a term that applies to a specific individual. Here, 'Zayd' is juz'i relative to 'man'. [Note: Stable; no significant shift.]
مشخصات (mushakhkhaṣāt): The individuating attributes that distinguish a particular individual in external existence, such as height, color, etc. [Note: Common term in logic and philosophy; no shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example given, why is 'man' called kulli and 'Zayd' called juz'i?
A) Because 'man' is a part of Zayd, and Zayd is the whole
B) Because 'man' encompasses all men, and Zayd is one of them
C) Because 'man' is the genus and Zayd is the species
D) Because 'man' is abstract and Zayd is concrete

Key Takeaway
The terms kulli and juz'i are relative; a term's status depends on what it is compared to, not on an inherent property.

The Five Universals

Classifying Universals: Essential, Accidental, or Species
Author defines dhati and aradi as universal predicates and distinguishes them from the species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any universal as essential, accidental, or species based on its relation to the quiddity of its individuals.

Core Idea
The essential-accidental distinction is not exhaustive: the species is a third category identical to the quiddity.

In this passage, the author explains why we need to distinguish essential from accidental universals: definitions (hudud) are built from essentials, while descriptions (rusum) use accidents. But he also reveals a third type—the species—which is neither a part nor outside the quiddity but identical to it. Understanding this threefold division is crucial for constructing sound definitions in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the universal term in question
Take any universal predicate (e.g., ‘animal’, ‘rational’, ‘laughing’, ‘man’). Ask: what quiddity (mahiyya) does it apply to? For this lesson, we focus on the quiddity of ‘man’ (insan).
The quiddity is the essence of the thing—what makes it what it is.
2
Determine its relation to the quiddity
Check whether the universal is (a) a part of the quiddity, (b) outside the quiddity, or (c) identical to the quiddity. A part is something like genus or differentia that, when combined, constitute the quiddity. Outside means not a constituent but a contingent attribute. Identical means it is the quiddity itself.
Use the passage's criteria: ‘if it enters’ means part; ‘if it exits’ means outside; if neither, it is the species.
3
Classify the universal
If it is a part, it is essential (dhati) and falls under genus or differentia. If it is outside, it is accidental (aradi) and is a common accident or property. If it is identical, it is the species (naw‘).
For example, ‘animal’ is a part (genus) → essential; ‘laughing’ is outside (property) → accidental; ‘man’ is identical → species.
4
Verify with examples from the passage
Test your classification against the author's examples: ‘animal’ for man is essential, ‘moving’ for man is common accident, ‘laughing’ for man is property, and ‘man’ for man is species.
This confirms that the division is threefold, not just essential vs. accidental.

فصل في مباحث الألفاظ

قَوْلُهُ : (٣٠) وَأَوَّلاً لِلذَّاتِ إِنْ فِيهَا انْدَرَجُ فَانْسِبْهُ، أَوْ لِعَارِضِ إِذَا خَرَجْ

لَمَّا كَانَتِ الكُلِّيَّاتُ مَبَادِئَ المُعَرِّفَاتِ حُدُوداً وَرُسُوماً ، وَكَانَتِ الحُدُودُ إِنَّمَا تَكُونُ بالذَّاتِيَّاتِ، وَالرُّسُومُ بالعَرَضِيَّاتِ احْتِيجَ إِلَى مَعْرِفَةِ الذَّاتِي وَالعَرَضِي، فَبَيَّنَهُمَا هُنَا ;

أَنَّ أَوَّلَ القِسْمَيْنِ السَّابِقَيْنِ وَهُوَ الكُلِّيُّ : ١ - يُنْسَبُ إِلَى الذَّاتِ ؛ أَي : المَاهِيَّةِ، فَيُقَالُ فِيهِ : ذَاتِيٌّ إِذَا كَانَ مُنْدَرِجاً فِيهَا ؛ أَيْ: جُزْءاً مِنْهَا . ٢ - وَيُنْسَبُ إِلَى العَرَضِ، فَيُقَالُ فِيهِ : عَرَضِيٌّ إِذَا كَانَ خَارِجاً عَنْهَا . ٣ - وَمَنْهُومُهُ : أَنَّهُ إِذَا لَمْ يَكُنْ جُزْءاً مِنَ المَاهِيَّةِ وَلَا خَارِجاً مِنْهَا؛ بِأَنْ كَانَ هُوَ نَفْسُ المَاهِيَّةِ، فَلَا يُقَالُ فِيهِ : ذَاتِيٌّ وَلَا عَرَضِيُّ، وَعَلَيْهِ فَالقِسْمَةُ ثُلَاثِيَّةٌ . فَالذَّاتِيُّ هُوَ: «الكُلِّيُّ الَّذِي يَكُونُ جُزْءاً مِنْ مَاهِيَّةِ أَفْرَادِهِ»، وَيَصْدُقُ بِالجِنْسِ كَ: «الحَيَوَانِ» لِـ «الإِنْسَانِ»، وَبِالفَصْلِ كَـ: «النَّاطِقِ» لَهُ . وَالعَرَضِيُّ هُوَ: «الكُلِّيُّ الخَارِجُ عَنْ مَاهِيَّةِ أَفْرَادِهِ»، وَيَصْدُقُ بِالعَرَضِ العَامِّ: «المُتَحَرِّكِ» لِـ «الإِنْسَانِ»، وَبِالعَرَضِ الخَاصِّ وَيُسَمَّى: «الخَاصَّةَ» كَـ: «الصَّاحِكِ» لِـ «الإِنْسَانِ». وَمَا لَيْسَ جُزْءاً وَلَا خَارِجاً هُوَ النَّوْعُ؛ كَـ: «الإِنْسَانِ»؛ لِأَنَّهُ نَفْسُ مَاهِيَّةِ أَفْرَادِهِ.
Chapter on Discussions of Words. His saying: 'And firstly, for the essence if in it it enters, so attribute it; or for the accident if it exits.' Since universals are the principles of definitions, both ḥudūd and rusūm, and ḥudūd are constituted by essentials, and rusūm by accidents, there is need for knowledge of the essential and the accidental, so he explained them here. That the first of the two preceding divisions, namely the universal: 1. is attributed to the essence (i.e., the quiddity), so it is called 'essential' if it is included in it; i.e., a part of it. 2. And it is attributed to the accident, so it is called 'accidental' if it is outside it. 3. And its meaning: that if it is neither a part of the quiddity nor outside it, but it is the quiddity itself, then it is not called essential nor accidental; accordingly, the division is threefold. The essential is: 'the universal that is a part of the quiddity of its individuals,' and applies to the genus like 'animal' for 'man', and to the differentia like 'rational' for him. The accidental is: 'the universal that is outside the quiddity of its individuals,' and applies to the common accident like 'moving' for 'man', and to the special accident (called the property) like 'laughing' for 'man'. And what is neither a part nor outside is the species; like 'man'; because it is the very quiddity of its individuals.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion of the matn line 'And firstly, for the essence if in it it enters...'. The author first explains why we need to distinguish essential (dhati) from accidental (aradi) universals: definitions (hudud) are built from essentials, descriptions (rusum) from accidents. Then he presents a threefold division of universals in relation to the quiddity (mahiyyah) of their individuals. (1) Dhati: a universal that is a part of the quiddity. Examples: genus (jins) like 'animal' for man; differentia (fasl) like 'rational' for man. (2) Aradi: a universal outside the quiddity. Examples: common accident (arad 'amm) like 'moving' for man; special accident/property (khassah) like 'laughing' for man. (3) A third type: the universal that is neither part nor outside but is the quiddity itself—this is the species (naw'), like 'man' itself. Thus the division is threefold, not merely twofold. The author uses standard Islamic examples: animal, rational, moving, laughing for man.

Key Takeaway
The essential-accidental classification is not a simple binary: the species is a third type that is identical to the quiddity, not a part or outside.

Three Views on the Division of Universals
Al-Bannani presents three scholarly views on the division of universals and resolves an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three scholarly views on the division of universals (twofold vs. threefold) and explain the responses to the objection about calling the species essential.

Core Idea
The classification of species as essential or accidental depends on interpreting 'essential' and 'dhāt'.

In Islamic logic, the division of universals into essential (dhātī) and accidental ('araḍī) is foundational for constructing definitions. However, scholars disagreed on where the species (al-naw') belongs. Al-Bannani presents three opinions and resolves a key objection about self-reference. Understanding these views sharpens your grasp of definitional precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three views
List the three opinions on dividing universals mentioned in the passage: (1) twofold division with species as accidental, (2) twofold division with species as essential, and (3) threefold division (essential, accidental, species).
The twofold views differ based on what is considered 'accidental' or 'essential'.
2
Understand the objection
The third view calls the species 'essential', which seems to relate a thing to itself (since the species is the quiddity). Why is that problematic?
The objection is that 'essential' implies a relation to essence, so species = essence leads to self-relation.
3
Analyze the first response
The yā' of relation may indicate exaggeration (mubālaghah), not actual relation. Example: 'aḥmarī' means 'extremely red', not 'related to red'. Similarly, 'dhātī' can mean 'very essence'.
This uses Arabic morphology to resolve the logical issue.
4
Analyze the second response
The term 'dhāt' can also refer to the individuals (maṣādiq) that the quiddity applies to. Thus, calling the species 'dhātī' means it relates to instances, not to itself.
This shifts the interpretation from universal to particular.
5
Evaluate the resolution
Both responses show how semantic flexibility in Arabic allows the third view to avoid contradiction. Which response do you find more convincing, and why?
Consider the context of Islamic logic and its reliance on linguistic analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مباحث الألفاظ (2) - وَبَعْضُهُمْ يَجْعَلُ القِسْمَةَ تُنَائِيَّةٌ فَقَط ، وَيَقُولُ: «العَرَضِيُّ هُوَ : مَا لَيْسَ جُزْءاً مِنَ المَاهِيَّةِ»، فَيَشْمَلُ النَّوْعَ . (3) - وَبَعضُهُمْ يَجْعَلُهَا تُنَائِيَّةٌ أَيْضاً ، وَيُدْخِلُ النَّوْعَ فِي الذَّاتِيِّ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ الذَّاتِيَّ هُوَ: «مَا لَيْسَ خَارِجاً عَنِ المَاهِيَّةِ». فَالأَقْوَالُ ثَلَاثَةٌ ; إِلَّا أَنَّ فِي تَسْمِيَةِ النَّوْعِ : ذَاتِيَّا» عَلَى الثَّالِثِ نِسْبَةُ الشَّيْءِ إِلَى نَفْسِهِ. وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ «يَاءَ» النِّسْبَةِ قَدْ تَلْحَقُ الأَسْمَاءَ لِلْمُبَالَغَةِ؛ نَحْوُ : «أَحْمَرِيٌّ» في «أَحْمَرَ) . وَقَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ : إِنَّ الذَّاتَ كَمَا تُطْلَقُ عَلَى الحَقِيقَةِ تُطْلَقُ عَلَى مَا صَدُقَاتِهَا، وَعَلَيْهِ تَكُونُ نِسْبَةُ النَّوْعِ إِلَيْهَا مِنْ نِسْبَةِ الحَقِيقَةِ إِلَى مَا صَدُقَاتِهَا .
Chapter on Topics of Words. (2) And some make the division merely twofold, and say: 'The accidental is what is not a part of the quiddity', so it includes the species. (3) And some make it also twofold, and include the species in the essential, based on the fact that the essential is 'what is not external to the quiddity'. So the opinions are three; except that in calling the species 'essential' according to the third opinion, it is a relationship of a thing to itself. And it is answered: that the yā' of relation may attach to nouns for amplification, like 'aḥmarī' in 'aḥmar' (red). And some said: that 'dhāt' is applied to the truth just as it is applied to what it applies to (individuals), and accordingly the relation of the species to it is like the relation of the truth to what it applies to.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage presents different scholarly views on the classification of universals. The author first notes that some scholars hold a twofold division: one group considers the species as part of the accidental (since it is not a part of the quiddity from a certain perspective), and another group considers it as essential (since it is not external to the quiddity). These two views, together with the threefold division, make three opinions. The author then addresses an objection: calling the species 'essential' in the third opinion would be a self-relation (since the species is the quiddity itself). Two responses are given: first, the yā' of relation can be used for exaggeration (mubālaghah), as in 'aḥmarī' meaning extremely red, so it does not imply a real relation. Second, 'dhāt' can also mean the individuals that the quiddity applies to, so the species relates to them as the truth relates to its instances. This shows the flexibility of terminology and the underlying conceptual precision.

يَاء النِّسْبَة
مُبَالَغَة

Key Takeaway
The three scholarly views on the division of universals reveal that terminology can be flexible, and objections resolved by linguistic or conceptual refinements.

Three Views on Essential vs Accidental Universals
Three opinions on classifying species as essential or accidental

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and contrast the three opinions on whether the species (naw') falls under essential (dhati) or accidental ('aradi) based on competing definitions.

Core Idea
The species is the quiddity itself, so its classification depends on how you define 'essential'.

This passage presents three ways to divide universals into essential and accidental, focusing on the status of the species. The debate hinges on two definitions: 'accidental is what is not part of the quiddity' and 'essential is what is not outside the quiddity'. By understanding these positions, you will see how subtle shifts in definition can reclassify a universal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Extract the definitions of 'essential (dhati)' and 'accidental ('aradi)' given in the passage. 'Accidental is what is not part of the quiddity' includes the species; 'Essential is what is not outside the quiddity' also includes the species. Note how each definition treats the species.
The definitions are mutually exclusive in extension only if 'mahiyyah' is interpreted differently.
2
Map each opinion to its classification of the species
First opinion: twofold division, accidental includes species. Second opinion: twofold division, essential includes species. Third opinion: acknowledges both but flags self-reference. Write out each position clearly.
The third opinion is not a new division but a recognition of a problem.
3
Compare the two definitions
Analyze why the same species can be classified differently. The first definition ('not part of mahiyyah') excludes the species because the species is the entire mahiyyah. The second ('not outside mahiyyah') includes it because the species is the mahiyyah itself.
The crux is whether 'part' means component or totality.
4
Test the definitions on an Islamic example
Apply this to the universal 'salat' (prayer). Is 'salat' a species? Its quiddity includes specific actions. According to the first definition, 'salat' as a species is accidental because it is the whole quiddity? Actually, need careful: the species is the quiddity, so by first definition it is not a part, thus accidental. By second definition, it is not outside the quiddity, so essential. See the difference.
This shows the practical stake for usul al-fiqh when defining legal categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وَبَعْضُهُمْ يَجْعَلُ القِسْمَةَ ثُنَائِيَّةً فَقَط، وَيَقُولُ: «العَرَضِيُّ هُوَ : مَا لَيْسَ جُزْءاً مِنَ المَاهِيَّةِ»، فَيَشْمَلُ النَّوْعَ . وَبَعضُهُمْ يَجْعَلُهَا ثُنَائِيَّةً أَيْضاً، وَيُدْخِلُ النَّوْعَ فِي الذَّاتِيِّ؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى أَنَّ الذَّاتِيَّ هُوَ: «مَا لَيْسَ خَارِجاً عَنِ المَاهِيَّةِ».
فَالأَقْوَالُ ثَلَاثَةٌ ; إِلَّا أَنَّ فِي تَسْمِيَةِ النَّوْعِ : «ذَاتِيَّا» عَلَى الثَّالِثِ نِسْبَةُ الشَّيْءِ إِلَى نَفْسِهِ. وَأُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ يَاءَ النِّسْبَةِ قَدْ تَلْحَقُ الأَسْمَاءَ لِلْمُبَالَغَةِ؛ نَحْوُ : «أَحْمَرِيٌّ» في «أَحْمَرَ». وَقَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ : إِنَّ الذَّاتَ كَمَا تُطْلَقُ عَلَى الحَقِيقَةِ تُطْلَقُ عَلَى مَا صَدُقَاتِهَا، وَعَلَيْهِ تَكُونُ نِسْبَةُ النَّوْعِ إِلَيْهَا مِنْ نِسْبَةِ الحَقِيقَةِ إِلَى مَا صَدُقَاتِهَا .
قصارة
Chapter on investigations of terms. Some make the division twofold only, and say: 'The accidental is: what is not a part of the quiddity', so it includes the species. And some make it twofold also, and include the species in the essential, based on that the essential is: 'what is not outside the quiddity'. So the opinions are three; except that in calling the species 'essential' according to the third is a relation of a thing to itself. And it was answered: by that the ya' of attribution may attach to nouns for exaggeration, like 'ahmariyy' in 'ahmar'. And some said: indeed 'dhat', just as it is applied to the reality, it is also applied to its instantiations; and accordingly the relation of the species to it is a relation of the reality to its instantiations.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage discusses the division of universals into essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) and presents three opinions. The first opinion makes a twofold division: the accidental is what is not a part of the quiddity, thus including the species. The second opinion also makes a twofold division but includes the species in the essential because the essential is defined as what is not outside the quiddity. The third opinion acknowledges that calling the species 'essential' leads to a self-reference (nisbah al-shay' ila nafsihi) since the species is the quiddity itself. Two answers are given: first, the ya' of attribution (like in 'ahmariyy' from 'ahmar') can indicate exaggeration, so 'dhati' means 'highly essential' rather than 'pertaining to essence'. Second, 'dhat' can refer to both the reality and its instantiations, so the species relates to 'dhat' as the reality relates to its instances, avoiding self-reference. The passage concludes with 'قصارة' (likely a summarizing remark).

The classification of the species as essential or accidental hinges on whether you define essential as 'not outside the quiddity' or accidental as 'not part of the quiddity'.

Resolving Self-Reference in Essential Universals
Two solutions to the self-reference problem of calling species essential

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain two solutions to the self-reference objection when calling the species 'essential': the linguistic solution (ya' of exaggeration) and the conceptual solution (dual meaning of 'dhat').

Core Idea
Language and polysemy can resolve logical paradoxes of self-reference.

The third opinion recognizes that calling the species 'essential' ('dhati') seems to be a self-reference (nisbah al-shay' ila nafsihi) because the species is the quiddity itself. The passage provides two elegant solutions: one from Arabic grammar (ya' al-nisbah for exaggeration) and one from semantics (the term 'dhat' can refer both to the reality and to its instantiations). This lesson unpacks these solutions and shows how they preserve logical consistency.

Guided Path
1
Understand the self-reference objection
The objection: If the species is the quiddity, then calling it 'dhati' (essential) is like saying 'the quiddity is quidditical' – a relation of a thing to itself. This seems redundant or paradoxical.
Self-reference is problematic because it appears to add nothing new.
2
Analyze the first answer: ya' of exaggeration
The ya' of attribution (ياء النسبة) can be used for exaggeration (mubalaghah), not just literal attribution. Example: 'ahmariyy' from 'ahmar' means 'very red' rather than 'having redness'. So 'dhati' means 'very essential' or 'the very essence' rather than 'pertaining to essence'. This avoids self-reference because it is a different kind of predication.
This is a linguistic solution grounded in Arabic morphology.
3
Analyze the second answer: dual meaning of 'dhat'
The word 'dhat' can refer to the reality (haqiqah) or to its instantiations (ma sadaqat). When we say the species is dhati, we mean it relates to 'dhat' as the reality relates to its instances. The species is the reality of its individuals, so the relation is not self-reference but reference to the species as a reality for those individuals.
This solution uses the polysemy of 'dhat' to dissolve the paradox.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the universal 'iman' (faith). If 'iman' is a species, calling it 'dhati' would be self-referential if 'dhat' means essence. But using the first solution, 'dhati' could mean 'very essential' (mubalaghah). Using the second, 'iman' as a species is the reality of individual instances of faith, so it is dhati in the sense of 'real' not 'self'. Test which solution fits better in kalam discussions.
This shows practical relevance in theological debates about essential attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The Twofold Division of Universals: Essential and Accidental
Commentator explains the meaning of the poem's lines defining essential and accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential and accidental universals based on whether they are part of the reality of a quiddity.

Core Idea
Essential universals are parts of the definition; accidental universals are not.

In this lesson, we examine the two couplets from al-Sullam al-Murawniq that define essential and accidental. The commentator explains the standard division: a universal is essential if it enters the reality of a thing (i.e., is a part of its definition), and accidental if it does not. We will learn to apply this criterion to various predicates from Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining couplets
Find the two couplets in the passage: the first from Shaykh Hamdun and the second from al-Qadiriyyah. They both state that essential is what is a part of reality, accidental is what is not.
2
Extract the definitions
Essential = a universal that enters the reality (i.e., is a part of the quiddity). Accidental = a universal that does not enter the reality. The second couplet uses 'jaza' haqiqah' (part of reality) explicitly.
The term 'reality' (haqiqah) here means the essence or quiddity.
3
Apply to Islamic examples
Consider the definition of 'human' as 'rational animal'. 'Animal' (genus) and 'rational' (differentia) are essential because they are parts of the reality of 'human'. 'Laughing' or 'writing' are accidental because they are not part of the definition.
Ensure examples are from Islamic sciences: e.g., in fiqh, the definition of 'salat' includes 'pillars' as essential, while 'sunan' are accidental.
4
Confirm the majority position
The commentator notes that this twofold division (essential/accidental) is the position of the majority (jumhur). Understand that this is the standard framework for analyzing definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (هَذَا هُوَ المَفْهُومُ مِنْ كَلَامِ النَّاظِمِ) وهو المشهور، وذلك لأنَّ النَّوع هو نفس الماهية والشَّيءُ لا يكون داخلاً في نفسه ولا خارجاً عنها، وإنما يتصوّر الدخول والخروج في الشَّي لا في غيره ؛ إذ لا بدَّ أن يتّصف بأحدهما بالنسبة إلى غيره، وعلى هذا درج شيخنا سيدي حمدور في «خريدته» فقال :
وَسَم بِالذَّاتِي كُلِّيَّا دَخَل حَقِيقَةٌ، وَالعَرَضِيُّ مَا عَدَل
قوله : (وَبَعْضُهُمْ يَجْعَلُ القِسْمَةَ ثُنَائِيَّةً) هذا مذهب الجمهور، وعليه دَرَج صاحب «القادريَّة»،
وَكُلُّ مَا جُزْء حَقِيقَةٍ جَلَا ذَاتِيٌّ ، وَإِلَّا عَرَضِيًّا اجْعَلَا
قوله : (لِلْمُبَالَغَةِ أي : بولغ في كون النوع ذاتيا وحقيقة تنبيهاً على أنَّه جزأها فقط، بل هو عينها ; لأنَّه لما اختصَّ عن الجنس والفصل بكونه تمام الماهيَّة بُولِغ في جوهر لفظه بزيادة ياء في آخره; مبالغة في معناه.
(١) هكذا هي في الطبعة الفاسية، وفي هامشها : ليس في نسخة الشارح . انتهى بخطه، وفي الطبعة الأميرية وضعت الفقرة بدون لفظة قوله» على أنها تتمة للقولة السابقة.
His saying: 'This is the understood from the speech of the versifier' and it is the well-known, and that because the species is the very quiddity and a thing is not inside itself nor outside it, but rather entering and exiting can only be conceived for a thing in relation to another; for it must be characterized by one of them in relation to another. And upon this proceeded our shaykh, sayyidi Hamdun, in his 'Kharidah', and he said: 'Name as essential every universal that entered the reality, and the accidental is what deviated.' His saying: 'And some make the division twofold' – this is the position of the majority, and upon it proceeded the author of 'al-Qadiriyyah'. 'And every thing that is a part of the reality is clear as essential, and if not, make it accidental.' His saying: 'for exaggeration' meaning: exaggeration was made in considering the species essential and reality as an alert that it is only its part, rather it is its very self; because when it is distinguished from the genus and differentia by being the complete quiddity, exaggeration was made in the substance of its word by adding a ya' at its end; an exaggeration in its meaning. (Footnote: Thus it is in the Fez edition, and in its margin: not in the copy of the commentator. End of his writing. And in the Amiri edition, the paragraph was placed without the word 'his saying' as a continuation of the previous statement.)
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two lines of the poem al-Sullam al-Murawniq. The commentator first explains that the species is the very quiddity itself, so the concept of being 'inside' or 'outside' the essence does not apply to it directly; these attributes only apply in relation to other things. Hence the species is called 'essential' in a well-known sense. He then quotes a couplet from Shaykh Hamdun's 'Kharidah': 'Name as essential every universal that entered the reality, and the accidental is what deviated.' This couplet gives a rule: essential = what enters the reality (i.e., a part of the definition), accidental = what does not. Next, he comments on the phrase 'some make the division twofold', attributing it to the majority and to the author of 'al-Qadiriyyah'. Another couplet follows: 'And every thing that is a part of the reality is clear as essential, and if not, make it accidental.' This is another expression of the same division. Finally, the commentator explains that the term 'dhati' (essential) when applied to the species involves exaggeration (mubalaghah), because the species is not just a part of the quiddity but its entirety; the ya' suffix indicates this exaggeration. A footnote notes a textual variant between editions.

Key Takeaway

Why the Species is Called Essential with Exaggeration
Commentator explains why species is called essential hyperbolically.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the species is called 'essential' hyperbolically even though it is the whole quiddity.

Core Idea
The species is the complete quiddity; calling it 'essential' is an exaggeration to highlight its role.

The commentator addresses a subtle point: the species is the entire quiddity, not a part. So how can it be called 'essential'? He explains that the term is used with exaggeration (mubalaghah), as indicated by the ya' suffix in 'dhati'. This lesson unpacks this nuance and its significance in the theory of definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the commentator's key point
The commentator states that the species is the very quiddity (al-naw' huwa nafs al-mahiyya). A thing cannot be inside or outside itself; so the concepts of 'entering' or 'being a part' do not directly apply.
2
Understand the problem
If essential means 'part of reality', how can the species, which is the whole reality, be called essential? The species is complete; it includes both genus and differentia. So it is not a mere part.
3
Identify the solution: exaggeration
The commentator explains that the species is called essential via exaggeration (mubalaghah). The ya' at the end of 'dhati' is a sign of exaggeration, like in 'nisbah' (attribution). It alerts us that the species is not just a part, but the very essence itself.
Compare with genus and differentia: they are truly essential as parts.
4
Reflect on the implication
This nuance shows that the term 'essential' can be used in a broader sense. In logic, we must be precise: when we say 'human' is the species, and 'animal' is essential, we mean different kinds of essentiality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And upon this proceeded our shaykh, sayyidi Hamdun, in his 'Kharidah', and he said: 'Name as essential every universal that entered the reality, and the accidental is what deviated.' His saying: 'And some make the division twofold' – this is the position of the majority, and upon it proceeded the author of 'al-Qadiriyyah'. 'And every thing that is a part of the reality is clear as essential, and if not, make it accidental.' His saying: 'for exaggeration' meaning: exaggeration was made in considering the species essential and reality as an alert that it is only its part, rather it is its very self; because when it is distinguished from the genus and differentia by being the complete quiddity, exaggeration was made in the substance of its word by adding a ya' at its end; an exaggeration in its meaning. (Footnote: Thus it is in the Fez edition, and in its margin: not in the copy of the commentator. End of his writing. And in the Amiri edition, the paragraph was placed without the word 'his saying' as a continuation of the previous statement.)
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two lines of the poem al-Sullam al-Murawniq. The commentator first explains that the species is the very quiddity itself, so the concept of being 'inside' or 'outside' the essence does not apply to it directly; these attributes only apply in relation to other things. Hence the species is called 'essential' in a well-known sense. He then quotes a couplet from Shaykh Hamdun's 'Kharidah': 'Name as essential every universal that entered the reality, and the accidental is what deviated.' This couplet gives a rule: essential = what enters the reality (i.e., a part of the definition), accidental = what does not. Next, he comments on the phrase 'some make the division twofold', attributing it to the majority and to the author of 'al-Qadiriyyah'. Another couplet follows: 'And every thing that is a part of the reality is clear as essential, and if not, make it accidental.' This is another expression of the same division. Finally, the commentator explains that the term 'dhati' (essential) when applied to the species involves exaggeration (mubalaghah), because the species is not just a part of the quiddity but its entirety; the ya' suffix indicates this exaggeration. A footnote notes a textual variant between editions.

Key Terms
ذاتي
عرضي
نوع
جنس
فصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, why is the ya' added at the end of 'dhati' when referring to the species?
A) To indicate the feminine gender.
B) To indicate exaggeration (mubalaghah) because the species is the whole quiddity.
C) To distinguish it from the accidental.
D) To make the word easier to pronounce.

Key Takeaway
The species is called essential hyperbolically because it is the entire quiddity, and the ya' suffix in 'dhati' signals this exaggeration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of 'salat' as 'specific sayings and actions, beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'. Identify what part of this definition represents the species (the whole essence) and what parts represent genus and differentia. Explain why the species itself is not a part but the complete reality.
?
Critique the statement: 'The species is a part of the definition of the thing.' Using the commentator's explanation, show why this statement is inaccurate and what the correct characterization should be.

Distinguishing Logical 'Aradi from Theological 'Arad
Author defines 'aradi and contrasts it with theological 'arad

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define logical 'aradi and differentiate it from theological 'arad, including the condition of predication.

Core Idea
Logical 'aradi is a universal predicated on the essence; theological 'arad is a contingent attribute without predication.

The term 'aradi appears in both logic and kalām, but with different meanings. This lesson clarifies the precise definition of 'aradi in logic and how it contrasts with the accident ('arad) of the theologians. Understanding this distinction prevents confusion when reading texts in either discipline.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definitions
Find in the Arabic the explicit definition of 'aradi according to logicians: 'what is accidental to the quiddity and predicated on it'. Also find the definition of 'arad according to theologians: 'the contingent attribute like whiteness' and note they do not require predication.
2
Identify the key difference
Notice that 'aradi is a universal (kulli) predicated on the subject, while 'arad is a particular attribute that inheres without being necessarily predicated. The logicians require predication; the theologians do not.
This is crucial for understanding how each discipline uses the term.
3
Apply the distinction to examples
The author gives 'white' predicated of 'animal' as an example of 'aradi. For 'arad, the example is 'whiteness' (a particular quality). Test whether 'whiteness' can be predicated of an animal in the same way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَدْ عُلِمَ أَنَّ العَرَضِيَّ عِنْدَ المَنَاطِقَةِ هُوَ : العَارِضُ لِلمَاهِيَّةِ المَحْمُولُ عَلَيْهَا كَـ : الأَبْيَض لِلْحَيَوَانِ، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ العَرَضِ عِنْدَ المُتَكَلِّمِينَ، بَلْ هُوَ : عِنْدَهُمُ الصِّفَةُ الحَادِثَةُ كَـ: «البَيَاضِ، وَلَا يَشْتَرِطُونَ حَمْلاً ، وَلَيْسَ فِي العَرَضِي عِنْدَ القَوْمِ نِسْبَةَ الشَّيْءِ إِلَى نَفْسِهِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ مِنْ نِسْبَةِ أَفْرَادِ الأَعَمِّ إِلَيْهِ، وَأَمَّا الذَّاتِيُّ فَمَنْسُوبٌ لِلذَّاتِ بِمَعْنَى الحَقِيقَةِ، لَا لِذَاتٍ بِمَعْنَى صَاحِبَةٍ ، وَلِكَوْنِهَا بِمَعْنَى الحَقِيقَةِ أُدْخِلَتْ عَلَيْهَا «أَلْ»، وَأُفْرِدَتْ عَنِ الإِضَافَةِ، وَاسْتِعْمَالُهَا بِهَذَا المَعْنَى لَيْسَ أَمْراً اصْطِلاحِيًّا فَقَط ، خِلَافاً لِلكَاتِبِي وَمَنْ تَبِعَهُ، بَلْ هُوَ مِنْ وَضْعِ اللُّغَةِ وَارِدٌ فِي كَلَامِ العَرَبِ؛ كَقَوْلِ سَيِّدِنَا خُبَيْبٍ رَضِيَ اللهُ تَعَالَى عَنْهُ : [من الطويل] وَذَلِكَ فِي ذَاتِ الإِلَهِ وَإِنْ يَشأ يُبَارِكْ عَلَى أَوْصَالِ شِلْوِ مُمَزَّعٍ
قوله : (وَقَدْ عُلِمَ لعله علم من قوله قبل في تعريفه : «هُوَ : الكلِّيُّ الخارج عن ماهية أفراده؛ لأنَّ الأفراد لا تكون أفراداً له إلَّا إذا صح أن يحمل عليها، وقد تبع الشارح في هذا الهلالي على عادته . والذي صرّح به اليوسي : أنَّ العَرَض عند هؤلاء القوم أعم من العرض عند المتكلمين، لا مباين له، وأمثلتهم له تُنبئك عن هذا العموم، فإنَّهم تارة يمثلون بالمصدر، وتارةً باسم الفاعل، والتّسامح خلافُ الأصل، وهذا الشارح نفسه مثل فيما تقدَّم بالضحك والتَّحرُّك على ما في بعض النسخ، وفي بعضها بالمتحرك والضَّاحك.
قوله : (وَلَيْسَ فِي العَرَضِيّ ... إلخ) جواب عن سؤال مقدَّرٍ تقديرُهُ : إذا كان العرض عندهم هو العارض للماهية المحمول عليها اتَّحد المنسوب والمنسوب إليه، فيكون من نسبة الشَّيء إلى نفسه. وأجيب : بمنع الاتحاد؛ لأنَّ المراد كلُّ فردٍ من أفراد العارض المحمول يقال له : «عَرَضِيٌّ»، فالمنسوب هو الفرد والمنسوبُ إليه النَّوع ، كما لو قسمت النَّاس إلى عربي وعجمي، فالمنسوب إليه - أعني : العرب والعجم - قسمان للنَّاس، فالمنسوب الفرد والمنسوب إليه النَّوع .
قوله : (وَذَلِكَ فِي ذَاتِ الإِلَهِ ما أشار إليه هذا الشارح من أنَّ الذَّات هنا بمعنى الحقيقة هو الذي في البخاري» في كتاب التَّوحيد، والذي اختاره الشَّارح السبكي والكرماني : أنَّ الذات هنا بمعنى الطاعة والسبيل .
(١) في (ب): «لِلْذَاتِ) بدلاً من لِذَاتِ) .
(٢) أخرجه البخاري (٣٠٤٥) من حديث أبي هريرة رضي الله تعالى عنه .
(٣) في (ب): زيادة: مَا أُبَالِي حِينَ أَقْتُلُ مُسْلِماً عَلَى أَي شِقٌ كَانَ لِلَّهِ مَصْرَعِي
And it has been known that the accidental ('aradi) according to the logicians is: what is accidental to the quiddity and predicated on it, like 'white' for 'animal'. And it is different from the accident ('arad) according to the theologians; rather it is, according to them, the contingent attribute like 'whiteness', and they do not require predication. And there is not in the 'aradi according to these people a relation of a thing to itself; because it is a relation of the individuals of the more general to it. And as for the essential (dhati), it is attributed to 'dhat' in the sense of reality, not to 'dhat' in the sense of possessor. And because it is in the sense of reality, the definite article 'al' was attached to it, and it was used without annexation. And its usage in this sense is not merely terminological, contrary to al-Katibi and those who follow him; rather it is from linguistic usage, occurring in the speech of the Arabs, like the saying of our master Khubayb, may Allah be pleased with him: [tawil] 'And that is in the essence of Allah, and if He wills He blesses the limbs of a torn body.'
His saying: 'and it has been known' perhaps it is known from his previous saying in its definition: 'It is the universal external to the quiddity of its individuals; because the individuals are only individuals for it if it is correct to predicate it of them.' And al-Hilali followed the commentator in this as is his habit. And what al-Yusi explicitly stated: that 'arad according to these people is more general than the accident according to the theologians, not completely different, and their examples indicate this generality: for sometimes they give examples with verbal nouns, sometimes with active participles. And tolerance is contrary to the principle, and this commentator himself previously gave examples like 'laughter' and 'movement' according to some manuscripts, and in others 'moving' and 'laughing'.
His saying: 'and there is not in the 'aradi...etc.' is an answer to an implied question, whose implication is: if the accident according to them is what is accidental to the quiddity and predicated on it, then the related and the related-to would be identical, resulting in a relation of a thing to itself. And it is answered: the identity is denied; because what is meant is that each individual of the accidental predicated is called ''aradi', so the related is the individual and the related-to is the species. Just as if you divided people into Arab and non-Arab, the related-to—namely Arab and non-Arab—are two divisions of people, so the related is the individual and the related-to is the species.
His saying: 'and that is in the essence of Allah' indicates that this commentator indicated that 'dhat' here means reality is what is in al-Bukhari, Kitab al-Tawhid; and what al-Subki and al-Kirmani chose is that 'dhat' here means obedience and the path.
(Footnote: In copy B: 'lil-dhati' instead of 'li-dhati'.
(2) Reported by al-Bukhari (3045) from Abu Hurayrah.
(3) In copy B: addition: 'I do not care if I am killed a Muslim, on which side I die for Allah's sake.'
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the term 'aradi (accidental universal) as used in logic, distinguishing it from 'arad (accident) in kalam. The logical 'aradi is defined as a universal that is accidental to the quiddity and predicated on it, e.g., 'white' predicated of 'animal'. The theologians' 'arad is a temporal attribute like 'whiteness', and they do not require predication. The author then addresses a potential objection: if 'aradi is defined as 'what is accidental to and predicated on the essence', then the term 'aradi would seemingly relate a thing to itself (since the same thing is both the subject and the predicate?). The answer is that this is avoided because 'aradi refers to the individuals of the universal, not the universal itself. For example, 'white' as a universal is 'aradi, and its individuals (like 'this white thing') are related to the species 'white'. So the relation is from the individual to the species, not a self-relation. Similarly, in dividing people into Arab and non-Arab, the terms 'Arab' and 'non-Arab' are species, and individual persons are related to them. The passage also contrasts dhati (essential), which is attributed to 'dhat' meaning the reality, not to 'dhat' meaning the possessor. The use of 'al-dhat' for reality is not purely terminological; it appears in classical Arabic, as in the verse of Khubayb. The author rebuts al-Katibi's claim that this usage is only technical. Further commentary notes that al-Yusi and others consider logical 'arad more general than the kalam accident, and examples show the range. The final note discusses the meanings of 'dhat' in the hadith. This passage is definitional and includes a refutation of an objection.

Key Terms
عرضي
عرض
مناطقة
متكلمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition do logicians add to the definition of 'aradi that theologians do not require for 'arad?
A) The attribute must be contingent.
B) The attribute must be predicated on the quiddity.
C) The attribute must be a universal.
D) The attribute must be temporal.

Key Takeaway
The logical 'aradi is a universal that is both accidental to and predicated on the quiddity, whereas the theological 'arad is a contingent attribute without the condition of predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find an example in fiqh where a ruling is predicated on a description (like 'wine is forbidden because it is intoxicating'). Identify the 'aradi (the universal 'intoxicant') and explain why it meets the logical definition of 'aradi.
?
Contrast with an example from kalam: consider the attribute of 'whiteness' in a white object. Explain why it is not an 'aradi according to logicians.

Why 'Aradi Does Not Involve Self-Reference
Author answers the self-reference objection to the definition of 'aradi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the definition of 'aradi does not lead to a self-referential relation, using the distinction between individuals and species.

Core Idea
'Aradi relates individuals of a universal to the species, not the universal to itself.

A potential objection arises: if 'aradi is defined as 'what is accidental to and predicated on the quiddity', then the term seems to relate a thing to itself. The author provides a clear answer, showing that 'aradi refers to the individuals of the universal, not the universal itself. This lesson breaks down the logical structure of the answer.

Guided Path
1
Identify the implied question
The objection is implicitly: if 'aradi is 'what is accidental to the quiddity and predicated on it', then the thing and the essence are the same, causing self-reference. Find this implied question in the text.
The text says: 'if the accident is what is accidental to the quiddity and predicated on it, then the related and the related-to would be identical, resulting in a relation of a thing to itself.'
2
Understand the answer: individuals vs species
The author denies identity: 'what is meant is that each individual of the accidental predicated is called 'aradi, so the related is the individual and the related-to is the species.' Recognize that 'aradi is the universal itself, but the relation is between its individuals and the universal.
Use the analogy of dividing people into Arab and non-Arab: the terms 'Arab' and 'non-Arab' are species, individuals are related to them.
3
Apply the distinction to a new example
Take the universal 'animal'. Its 'aradi (e.g., 'white') is predicated of individual animals. Show why this is not a self-relation: 'white' (the universal) is related to its individuals, but the definition of 'aradi relates individuals to the species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the term 'aradi (accidental universal) as used in logic, distinguishing it from 'arad (accident) in kalam. The logical 'aradi is defined as a universal that is accidental to the quiddity and predicated on it, e.g., 'white' predicated of 'animal'. The theologians' 'arad is a temporal attribute like 'whiteness', and they do not require predication. The author then addresses a potential objection: if 'aradi is defined as 'what is accidental to and predicated on the essence', then the term 'aradi would seemingly relate a thing to itself (since the same thing is both the subject and the predicate?). The answer is that this is avoided because 'aradi refers to the individuals of the universal, not the universal itself. For example, 'white' as a universal is 'aradi, and its individuals (like 'this white thing') are related to the species 'white'. So the relation is from the individual to the species, not a self-relation. Similarly, in dividing people into Arab and non-Arab, the terms 'Arab' and 'non-Arab' are species, and individual persons are related to them. The passage also contrasts dhati (essential), which is attributed to 'dhat' meaning the reality, not to 'dhat' meaning the possessor. The use of 'al-dhat' for reality is not purely terminological; it appears in classical Arabic, as in the verse of Khubayb. The author rebuts al-Katibi's claim that this usage is only technical. Further commentary notes that al-Yusi and others consider logical 'arad more general than the kalam accident, and examples show the range. The final note discusses the meanings of 'dhat' in the hadith. This passage is definitional and includes a refutation of an objection.

نسبة الشيء إلى نفسه

D) Because 'aradi is a universal.

Key Takeaway
'Aradi avoids self-reference because it relates individuals of the universal to the species, not the universal to itself.

The Linguistic Basis of 'Dhat' for Reality
Author defends the linguistic usage of 'dhat' for reality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two meanings of 'dhat' (essence vs. possessor) and provide evidence from the Arabic language that 'dhat' can mean reality, refuting al-Katibi's claim of mere terminology.

Core Idea
'Dhat' in Arabic means reality, not just possessor, and this is grounded in classical usage, not just technical jargon.

In logical discussions, the term 'dhat' is used to mean the reality or quiddity of a thing. Some like al-Katibi claimed this is a purely technical convention. The author rebuts this by citing classical Arabic, including a verse by the companion Khubayb. This lesson explores how language supports logical definitions.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two senses of 'dhat'
The passage distinguishes between 'dhat' meaning reality (haqiqah) and 'dhat' meaning the possessor (sahib). Find where the author says: 'al-dhati is attributed to dhat in the sense of reality, not to dhat in the sense of possessor'.
2
Understand the linguistic evidence
The author argues that this usage is not merely terminological (as al-Katibi thought) but is rooted in Arabic. The proof is the verse of Khubayb: 'وذلك في ذات الإله' – meaning 'that is in the essence of Allah'. Identify how this supports the meaning of reality.
3
Contrast with al-Katibi's position
Al-Katibi claimed that using 'dhat' for reality is purely technical. The author refutes this by showing pre-Islamic and Islamic Arabic usage. Understand why this linguistic grounding matters for logical definitions.
If the term were purely technical, its meaning could be arbitrary; linguistic grounding ensures stability.

And it has been known that the accidental ('aradi) according to the logicians is: what is accidental to the quiddity and predicated on it, like 'white' for 'animal'. And it is different from the accident ('arad) according to the theologians; rather it is, according to them, the contingent attribute like 'whiteness', and they do not require predication. And there is not in the 'aradi according to these people a relation of a thing to itself; because it is a relation of the individuals of the more general to it. And as for the essential (dhati), it is attributed to 'dhat' in the sense of reality, not to 'dhat' in the sense of possessor. And because it is in the sense of reality, the definite article 'al' was attached to it, and it was used without annexation. And its usage in this sense is not merely terminological, contrary to al-Katibi and those who follow him; rather it is from linguistic usage, occurring in the speech of the Arabs, like the saying of our master Khubayb, may Allah be pleased with him: [tawil] 'And that is in the essence of Allah, and if He wills He blesses the limbs of a torn body.'
His saying: 'and it has been known' perhaps it is known from his previous saying in its definition: 'It is the universal external to the quiddity of its individuals; because the individuals are only individuals for it if it is correct to predicate it of them.' And al-Hilali followed the commentator in this as is his habit. And what al-Yusi explicitly stated: that 'arad according to these people is more general than the accident according to the theologians, not completely different, and their examples indicate this generality: for sometimes they give examples with verbal nouns, sometimes with active participles. And tolerance is contrary to the principle, and this commentator himself previously gave examples like 'laughter' and 'movement' according to some manuscripts, and in others 'moving' and 'laughing'.
His saying: 'and there is not in the 'aradi...etc.' is an answer to an implied question, whose implication is: if the accident according to them is what is accidental to the quiddity and predicated on it, then the related and the related-to would be identical, resulting in a relation of a thing to itself. And it is answered: the identity is denied; because what is meant is that each individual of the accidental predicated is called ''aradi', so the related is the individual and the related-to is the species. Just as if you divided people into Arab and non-Arab, the related-to—namely Arab and non-Arab—are two divisions of people, so the related is the individual and the related-to is the species.
His saying: 'and that is in the essence of Allah' indicates that this commentator indicated that 'dhat' here means reality is what is in al-Bukhari, Kitab al-Tawhid; and what al-Subki and al-Kirmani chose is that 'dhat' here means obedience and the path.

(2) Reported by al-Bukhari (3045) from Abu Hurayrah.
(3) In copy B: addition: 'I do not care if I am killed a Muslim, on which side I die for Allah's sake.'

Defining 'Aradi: Accidental Attribute in Logic vs. Theology
Author defines 'aradi and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the logicians' 'aradi from the theologians' 'arad and explain why 'aradi does not involve self-reference.

Core Idea
The logicians' 'aradi is an accidental predicate, not a temporal accident.

In the study of the five universals, the term 'aradi (accidental) has a precise technical meaning among logicians. This passage clarifies that 'aradi refers to an attribute that is predicated on an essence, such as 'white' said of 'animal'. It is not the same as the theologians' 'arad, which is a temporal attribute. Moreover, the author addresses a subtle objection: if 'aradi is an attribute of the essence, doesn't that make it a relation of a thing to itself? He resolves this by showing that the relation is between individuals and species, not identity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions being contrasted
Locate the definitions of 'aradi according to logicians and 'arad according to theologians. What is the key difference?
The logicians require predication; theologians do not.
2
Understand the logical relation involved
In the logicians' definition, 'aradi is both 'arid (accidental) and mahmul (predicated). Why does this raise an objection about self-reference?
The objection arises because the attribute and the essence might seem identical in relation.
3
Examine the objection and answer
The imagined objection claims that 'aradi relates a thing to itself. How does the author refute this? What is the actual relation?
The author denies unification: the relatum is the individual predicate instance, the related is the species.
4
Apply the distinction to an example
The author uses the division of people into Arab and non-Arab. Explain how this example illustrates that the relatum is the individual and the related is the species.
Just as 'Arab' is a category for individuals, 'aradi is a category applied to individuals of a species.

وَقَدْ عُلِمَ أَنَّ العَرَضِيَّ عِنْدَ المَنَاطِقَةِ هُوَ : العَارِضُ لِلمَاهِيَّةِ المَحْمُولُ عَلَيْهَا كَـ : الأَبْيَض لِلْحَيَوَانِ، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ العَرَضِ عِنْدَ المُتَكَلِّمِينَ، بَلْ هُوَ : عِنْدَهُمُ الصِّفَةُ الحَادِثَةُ كَـ: «البَيَاضِ، وَلَا يَشْتَرِطُونَ حَمْلاً ، وَلَيْسَ فِي العَرَضِي عِنْدَ القَوْمِ نِسْبَةَ الشَّيْءِ إِلَى نَفْسِهِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ مِنْ نِسْبَةِ أَفْرَادِ الأَعَمِّ إِلَيْهِ، وَأَمَّا الذَّاتِيُّ فَمَنْسُوبٌ لِلذَّاتِ بِمَعْنَى الحَقِيقَةِ، لَا لِذَاتٍ بِمَعْنَى صَاحِبَةٍ. قوله : (وَلَيْسَ فِي العَرَضِيّ ... إلخ) جواب عن سؤال مقدَّرٍ تقديرُهُ : إذا كان العرض عندهم هو العارض للماهية المحمول عليها اتَّحد المنسوب والمنسوب إليه، فيكون من نسبة الشَّيء إلى نفسه. وأجيب : بمنع الاتحاد؛ لأنَّ المراد كلُّ فردٍ من أفراد العارض المحمول يقال له : «عَرَضِيٌّ»، فالمنسوب هو الفرد والمنسوبُ إليه النَّوع ، كما لو قسمت النَّاس إلى عربي وعجمي، فالمنسوب إليه - أعني : العرب والعجم - قسمان للنَّاس، فالمنسوب الفرد والمنسوب إليه النَّوع .
And it has been known that al-‘araḍī according to the logicians is: what is accidental to the essence and predicated upon it, like ‘white’ for ‘animal’. And it is different from al-‘araḍ according to the theologians; rather, it is, according to them, the temporal attribute, like ‘whiteness’, and they do not require predication. And there is no relation of a thing to itself in al-‘araḍī according to the people; because it is from the relation of individuals of the more general to it. And as for al-dhātī, it is attributed to al-dhāt in the sense of reality, not to a dhāt in the sense of a possessor. His statement: 'And there is not in al-‘araḍī... etc.' is an answer to an implied question, whose estimation is: If al-‘araḍ according to them is what is accidental to the essence and predicated upon it, then the relatum and the related would be one, so it would be from the relation of a thing to itself. And it was answered: by denying the unification; because what is meant is that every individual of the individuals of the accidental predicate is called ‘araḍī. So the relatum is the individual and the related is the species, just as if you divided people into Arab and non-Arab, the related—meaning Arabs and non-Arabs—are two divisions of people, so the relatum is the individual and the related is the species.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'aradi (accidental) as used by logicians and distinguishes it from the theologians' term 'arad (accident). The logicians' 'aradi is an attribute that is accidental to the essence (mahiyya) and is predicated upon it, like 'white' said of 'animal'. The theologians, by contrast, use 'arad to mean a temporal attribute (sifa haditha) like whiteness itself, without requiring predication. The author then addresses a potential objection: if 'aradi is an attribute of the essence, wouldn't it be a relation of a thing to itself? The answer is no, because the relation is between the individual instance of the predicate and the species, not between the essence and itself. An example: dividing people into Arab and non-Arab — the individual belongs to the category, but the category is not self-related. The commentary (qawluh) explains that the objection arises from assuming a unified relatum, but the relatum is the individual and the related is the species, so no self-reference occurs.

The Linguistic Basis of 'Dhat' as Reality: A Refutation of al-Katibi
Author argues that 'dhat' meaning haqiqa is linguistic, citing evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the linguistic evidence cited by Bajuri to prove that 'dhat' means 'reality' in Arabic, and explain why al-Katibi's claim that it is only technical is refuted.

Core Idea
Dhat means haqiqa in Arabic, not just in logic: the Quran, poetry, and dictionaries prove it.

This passage from Bajuri's commentary defends the Arabic usage of the word 'dhat' to mean 'reality' (haqiqa). The logician al-Katibi had claimed this was only a technical convention, but Bajuri shows it is deeply rooted in the language, citing the Quran, the poetry of the companion Khubayb, and the dictionary al-Qamus. Understanding this helps us see how logical terms often have a pre-existing linguistic underpinning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim being refuted
Al-Katibi and his followers asserted that using 'dhat' to mean 'reality' is a technical convention of the logicians, not a genuine Arabic usage. This is the target of the author's argument.
The author explicitly says 'khilāfan li-l-Kātibī wa man tabi'ahu'.
2
Find the linguistic evidence
The author presents three pieces of evidence: (1) the poetry of Khubayb ibn 'Adiyy using 'dhāt al-ilāh' (the reality of God), (2) the Quranic verse 'wa aṣliḥū dhāta baynikum' (interpreted as 'the reality of your connection'), and (3) the dictionary al-Qāmūs which records this usage.
Notice how the author uses both poetic and Quranic examples, showing the word's presence in the highest registers of Arabic.
3
Understand the grammatical clues
The author notes that when 'dhat' means 'reality', it takes the definite article 'al-' and is used without a following genitive (idafa). This grammatical behavior distinguishes it from other uses (like 'self').
E.g., 'al-dhāt' with 'al-' signals it is a noun in its own right.
4
Notice the morphological exception
The relative adjective 'dhātī' is irregular: it does not follow the standard pattern for nisba formation. The author explains that this is due to the 'tā'' being considered part of the root, which is contrary to the apparent form.
This is a side note, but it shows the author's attention to linguistic regularity.
5
Summarize the argument
The author refutes al-Katibi by providing direct textual evidence from classical Arabic sources (poetry, Quran, dictionary), demonstrating that the meaning of 'dhat' as 'reality' is linguistic, not merely technical.
The evidence is authoritative and sufficient to rebut the claim.

وَلِكَوْنِهَا بِمَعْنَى الحَقِيقَةِ أُدْخِلَتْ عَلَيْهَا «أَلْ»، وَأُفْرِدَتْ عَنِ الإِضَافَةِ، وَاسْتِعْمَالُهَا بِهَذَا المَعْنَى لَيْسَ أَمْراً اصْطِلاحِيًّا فَقَط ، خِلَافاً لِلكَاتِبِي وَمَنْ تَبِعَهُ، بَلْ هُوَ مِنْ وَضْعِ اللُّغَةِ وَارِدٌ فِي كَلَامِ العَرَبِ؛ كَقَوْلِ سَيِّدِنَا خُبَيْبٍ رَضِيَ اللهُ تَعَالَى عَنْهُ : (۲)(۳) [من الطويل] وَذَلِكَ فِي ذَاتِ الإِلَهِ وَإِنْ يَشأ يُبَارِكْ عَلَى أَوْصَالِ شِلْوِ مُمَزَّعٍ. وَمِنْهُ قَوْلُهُ تَعَالَى: ﴿ وَأَصْلِحُوا ذَاتَ بَيْنِكُمْ ﴾ [الأنفال: 1] أَيْ : حَقِيقَةً وَصْلِكُمْ، وَعَلَيْهِ دَرَجَ فِي القَامُوسِ» (١) ، وَبِذَلِكَ رَدَّ عَلَى مَنْ أَنْكَرَ وُرُودَهَا بِهَذَا المَعْنَى ، غَيْرَ أَنَّ قَوْلَهُمْ فِي النَّسَبِ إِلَيْهَا : ذَاتِيٌّ» غَيْرُ جَارٍ عَلَى القِيَاسِ؛ إِلَّا إِنْ جُعِلَتِ «التَّاءُ» أَصْلِيَّةٌ، وَهُوَ خِلَافُ الظَّاهِرِ.
And because it (i.e., dhāt) is in the sense of reality, the definite article 'al-' is attached to it, and it is used without idāfa (genitive construction). And its usage in this sense is not merely a technical matter, contrary to al-Kātibī and those who follow him. Rather, it is from the linguistic convention and occurs in the speech of the Arabs, like the statement of our master Khubayb, may Allāh be pleased with him: [verse] 'And that is concerning the dhāt of God, and if He wills, He blesses the limbs of a torn body.' And from it is His statement, exalted: {And make peace concerning the dhāt of your affairs} (Qur'ān 8:1), meaning: the reality of your connection. And upon this al-Qāmūs (the dictionary) proceeded. And by that he responded to those who denied its occurrence in this meaning, except that their formation of the nisba (relative adjective) to it as 'dhātī' is not according to analogy, unless the 'tā'' is considered radical, which is contrary to the apparent.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage is a defense of the linguistic usage of the term dhāt to mean 'reality' (haqīqa). The author argues that this is not just a technical convention of the logicians, as al-Kātibī claimed, but is rooted in the Arabic language. Evidence is given from the poetry of Khubayb ibn 'Adiyy (a companion of the Prophet) and from the Qur'ān (8:1): 'wa aṣliḥū dhāta baynikum,' interpreted as 'the reality of your connection.' The author also references al-Qāmūs (the famous Arabic dictionary). However, he notes that the relative adjective (nisba) from dhāt is dhātī, which is not formed on the standard analogy because the tā' would have to be considered part of the root (aṣliyya), which is not the apparent case. This is a side note on Arabic morphology.

The word 'dhat' is used in classical Arabic to mean 'reality' (haqiqa), as proven by the Quran, poetry, and lexicons, refuting the claim that this is merely a technical convention.

The Five Universals

First Warning: Distinguishing Essential from Accidental with Three Ancient Distinctions
Clarifies confusion between essential and accidental in referent, refutes terminological claim, presents three distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the three ancient distinctions (inseparability from essence, non-illatability, and Ibn Sina's priority test) to determine whether a given attribute is essential (dhati) or accidental (aradi) in an Islamic logical context.

Core Idea
The essential/accidental distinction is rooted in reality, not convention; the three tests reveal it.

In this passage, the author warns that even when we know the conceptual difference between essential and accidental, we can still be confused about which is which when applying them to real things. He refutes the idea that the distinction between differentia (fasl) and proper accident (khassa) is merely terminological. Instead, he presents three distinctions from the ancients (Ghazali and Ibn Sina) that serve as practical tests to determine whether a predicate is essential or accidental. These tests are grounded in the nature of the quiddity, not in arbitrary terminology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the confusion in masduq (referent)
The passage warns that although the concepts (mafhum) of essential and accidental are clear, people confuse them in their referents (masduq). This is why the difference between differentia and proper accident seems ambiguous. Recognize that the problem is not the concepts but their application.
This confusion leads some to mistakenly think the distinction is merely conventional.
2
Refute the terminological claim
The author argues that being part of the quiddity is a matter of reality, not naming. Only the technical names 'fasl' and 'khassa' are conventional. The claimant's intention was pedagogical, not ontological relativism.
The commentary clarifies that the claimant meant they used the designation for teaching purposes, not that the reality is arbitrary.
3
Apply the first distinction: inseparability from essence
Essential attributes are those without which the essence cannot be understood. For example, 'animal' cannot be removed from the conception of 'human'; therefore it is essential. 'Laughing' can be removed, so it is accidental.
Test: Try to think of the species without the attribute. If you cannot, it is essential.
4
Apply the second distinction: non-'illatability
Essential attributes cannot be explained by a cause. Asking 'why is human rational?' is like asking 'why is human human?' Accidental attributes like 'laughing' have a cause (e.g., tickling).
If the question 'why does X have attribute Y?' is answerable, the attribute is accidental; if it reduces to identity, it is essential.
5
Integrate Ibn Sina's test (third distinction)
Ibn Sina states that the essential is that which must be understood first to understand the essence. For human, you must first understand 'animal' to understand 'human'. This reinforces the first distinction.
This test formalizes the idea of conceptual priority.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيهان: V (۱) - الأَوَّلُ : قَدْ عَلِمْتَ الفَرْقَ بَيْنَ الذَّاتِي وَالعَرَضِي مِنْ حَيْثُ مَفْهُومُهُمَا ، وَكَثِيراً مَا يَلْتَبِسَانِ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَصْدُوقُ، وَمِنْ هُنَا يَشْكُلُ الفَرْقُ بَيْنَ الفَصْلِ وَالخَاصَّةِ، حَتَّى زَعَمَ بَعْضُهُمْ أَنَّ تَفْرِيقَهُمْ بَيْنَ النَّاطِقِ وَالصَّاحِكِ حَيْثُ جَعَلُوا الأَوَّلَ فَضْلاً وَالتَّعْرِيفَ بِهِ حَدًّا ، وَالثَّانِي خَاصَّةٌ وَالتَّعْرِيفَ بِهِ رَسْماً إِنَّمَا هُوَ اصْطِلاحِيٌّ فَقَط . قوله : (وَكَثِيراً مَا يَلْتَبِسَانِ مِنْ حَيْثُ المَصْدُوقُ) أي : بأن لا يدري في بعض الكليات أهو داخل في الماهية فيحكم عليه بأنه ذاتي، أو خارج فيحكم عليه بأنَّه عرضي؟ إذ كثيراً ما يشتبه الجنس بالعَرَض العام، والفصلُ بالخاصَّة على الأذكياء النَّحارير فضلاً عن غيرهم. وَلَيْسَ كَمَا زَعَمَ ، فَإِنَّ كَوْنَ الشَّيْءِ جُزْءاً مِنَ الحَقِيقَةِ، أَوْ خَارِجَا عَنْهَا أَمْرٌ ثَابِتٌ فِي الوَاقِعِ لَيْسَ رَاجِعاً لِلاصْطِلاحِ . نَعَمْ؛ التَّسْمِيَةُ بِالفَصْلِ وَالخَاصَّةِ أَمْرُ اصْطِلاحِيٌّ ، وَلَكِنْ لَيْسَ الكَلَامُ فِيهَا ، وَقَدْ فَرَّقَ بَيْنَهُمَا القُدَمَاءُ بِثَلَاثَةِ فُرُوقٍ : الأَوَّلُ : أَنَّ الذَّاتِي هُوَ الَّذِي لَا يُمْكِنُ فَهْمُ الذَّاتِ بِدُونِهِ، وَالعَرَضِيَّ بِخِلَافِهِ . الثَّانِي: أَنَّ الذَّاتِي هُوَ الَّذِي لَا يُعَلَّلُ، وَالعَرَضِيَّ يُعَلَّلُ. قوله : (وَلَيْسَ كَمَا زَعَمَ ليس ما فهمه هذا الشَّارح هو مراد هذا القائل، كما أفصح عن ذلك شيخنا المحقق سيّدي الطَّيِّب، فقال : إنَّ مراد هذا القائل أنَّهم لم يتحقَّقوا أنَّ الناطق جزء الحقيقة، والضاحك خارج عنها بحسب الواقع ونفس الأمر، فاصطلحوا على اعتقاد أنَّ الناطق داخل والضاحك خارج قصداً للتَّقريب والتَّمثيل، وليس مراد هذا القائل : أنَّ كون الشَّيء ذاتيًّا أو عَرَضب هو بحسب الاصطلاح ؛ إذ لا يقول به مميز فضلاً عن متميز . قوله : (وَقَدْ فَرَّقَ . .. إلخ) هذه الفروق الثلاثة ذكرها الإمام أبو حامد الغزالي في كتاب «مقاصد الفلاسفة»، انظر نصَّه في حواشي أبي حفص على «المختصر». قوله : (أَنَّ الذَّاتِي هُوَ الَّذِي لَا يُمْكِنُ ... إلخ) قال أبو علي بن سينا : الذاتي ما إذا فهم معناه وأخطر بالبال وفهم معنى ما هو ذاتي له وأخطر بالبال معه لم يمكن أن نفهم ذات الموصوف إلَّا بعد فهم ذلك المعنى أوَّلاً كـ الإنسان والحيوان، فإنَّك إذا فهمت ما الحيوان، وفهمت ما الإنسان، فلا تفهم الإنسان إلا وقد فهمت أولاً أنَّه حيوان، وأما ما ليس ذاتيا فقد تفهم ذات الموصوف مجرَّداً دونه ك كونه أبيض، أو موجوداً مثلاً . قوله : (أَنَّ الذَّاتِي هُوَ الَّذِي لَا يُعَلَّلُ) أي : لا يثبت لما هو ذاتي له بعِلَّةٍ، فلا تقول: «لِمَ كان الإنسان حيواناً أو ناطقاً ؟»؛ لأنَّ الإنسان هو كذلك، فكأنَّك قلت: «لِمَ كان الإنسان إنساناً؟». وكذا : السَّواد مثلاً لون لذاته، لا لشيء آخر يجعله لوناً ، فلا تقول: «لم كان لوناً؟»، بخلاف الضحك، فإنَّه يثبت للإنسان بعلَّةٍ .
Two warnings: The first: You have known the difference between essential (dhati) and accidental (aradi) in terms of their concepts (mafhum). But often they are confused in terms of their referent (masduq). From here, the distinction between differentia (fasl) and proper accident (khassa) becomes problematic, to the extent that some claim that their differentiation between 'rational' (natiq) and 'laughing' (dahik) — making the first a differentia and definition by it a definition (hadd), and the second a proper accident and definition by it a description (rasm) — is merely terminological (istilahi). And it is not as they claimed, for the thing being part of the reality or outside it is a matter fixed in reality, not referring to terminology. Yes, the naming as differentia and proper accident is a terminological matter, but the discussion is not about that. The ancients made three distinctions between them: First: The essential is that without which the essence cannot be understood, contrary to the accidental. Second: The essential is that which is not 'illatable (cannot be asked why), while the accidental is 'illatable. ... (then commentary) ... His statement: (And it is not as they claimed) — what this commentator understood is not the intent of that claimant, as our verifying master Sayyid al-Tayyib clarified: the claimant meant that they had not verified that 'rational' is part of the reality and 'laughing' is outside it according to reality and the matter itself, so they terminologically agreed on the belief that 'rational' is inside and 'laughing' is outside for the purpose of approximation and illustration. It is not the claimant's intent that the thing being essential or accidental is according to terminology; for no rational person would say that, let alone a distinguished one. His statement: (And they distinguished ...) — these three distinctions were mentioned by Imam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali in his book Maqasid al-Falasifa. See his text in the footnotes of Abu Hafs on al-Mukhtasar. His statement: (The essential is that without which...) — Abu Ali ibn Sina said: The essential is that when you understand its meaning and bring it to mind, and understand the meaning of what it is essential for and bring it to mind with it, you cannot understand the essence of the described except after understanding that meaning first. For example, 'human' and 'animal': when you understand what animal is, and understand what human is, you do not understand human unless you have first understood that it is an animal. As for that which is not essential, you may understand the essence of the described without it, like being white, or existing, for example. His statement: (The essential is that which is not 'illatable) — meaning it is not established for that which it is essential for by a cause. So you do not say: 'Why is human an animal or rational?' because human is so; it is as if you said: 'Why is human human?' Similarly, black is a color by itself, not because of another thing that makes it a color, so you do not say: 'Why is it a color?' contrary to laughing, for it is established for human by a cause.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a warning (tanbih) about a common confusion in logic. The author begins by reminding that essential (dhati) and accidental (aradi) differ in concept, but often they are confused in terms of their referent (masduq). This confusion leads some to mistakenly think that the difference between differentia (fasl) and proper accident (khassa) is purely conventional. They point to examples like 'natiq' (rational) as a differentia and 'dahik' (laughing) as a proper accident, claiming the distinction is only terminological. The author refutes this: whether something is part of the quiddity (mahiyya) or outside it is a matter of reality, not convention. Only the naming (tasmiya) as differentia or proper accident is conventional. He then presents three distinctions made by the ancients (cited from al-Ghazali and Ibn Sina). The first distinction: the essential is that without which the essence cannot be understood, while the accidental can be removed from the conception of the essence. Example: animal is essential to human, but whiteness is not. The second distinction: the essential is not 'illatable — one cannot ask 'why' it is predicated, because it is intrinsic, like asking 'why is human human?' The accidental, on the other hand, has a cause, like laughing in humans. The commentary elaborates: Ibn Sina defines essential as that which must be understood first to grasp the essence, using human and animal as an example. The passage also clarifies the intent of the claimant who said the distinction is terminological: it was meant as a pedagogical approximation, not a denial of the reality. The three distinctions are attributed to al-Ghazali, and the text provides Ibn Sina's detailed explanation. The passage reinforces that the essential/accidental distinction is rooted in the nature of things, not arbitrary convention.

Essential attributes are those that form part of the quiddity and are not subject to explanatory causation; they are discovered by testing whether they are inseparable from the conception of the essence and whether they can be asked 'why'.

beginner
