The Third Definition of Essential and Accidental
Author defines the third definition of dhati and 'aradi with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a property is essential or accidental using the third definition: assume its removal—if the essence cannot remain, it is essential.

Core Idea
Essential: if you assume it removed, the essence vanishes; accidental: it does not.

The author now gives a powerful test: essential properties are those whose removal, even in assumption, destroys the essence. This goes beyond mere conceptual priority or causality. The example of 'rational' for man shows that without rationality, the quiddity of man cannot be conceived, whereas 'laughing' is accidental because man can be conceived without it.

Guided Path
1
Understand the third definition
The essential property is that which, if you assume it removed from the essence, the essence would not remain. The accidental is the opposite; its removal does not affect the essence.
This is a conceptual test: we only assume removal, not actually remove it.
2
Apply the test to 'rational' (natiq) for man
The text says: it is not possible to understand the quiddity of man without rationality. If we assume rationality removed, the essence of man would vanish.
Rationality is thus essential for man.
3
Apply the test to 'laughing' (dahim) for man
It is possible to understand the quiddity of man without laughing; man remains man even if he never laughs. Therefore laughing is accidental.
Laughing is caused by a chain: wonder, perception, rationality, so it is dependent.
4
Note the causal asymmetry
Rationality is uncaused with respect to the essence, while laughing is caused by rationality. This reinforces the essential-accidental distinction.
Essential properties are not caused by other properties of the same essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّالِثُ : أَنَّ الذَّاتِي هُوَ الَّذِي لَا تَبْقَى الذَّاتُ مَعَ تَوَهُم رَفْعِهِ، وَالعَرَضِيَّ بِخِلَافِهِ؛ مِثَالُهُ : النَّاطِقُ ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يُمْكِنُ فَهُمُ مَاهِيَّةِ الإِنْسَانِ بِدُونِهِ، وَيُمْكِنُ فَهْمُهَا بِدُونِ «ضَاحِكِ»، كَمَا أَنَّهُ إِذَا ثَبَتَ لَهَا بِلَا عِلَّةٍ، وَالصَّاحِكُ مَعْلُولٌ بِالتَّعَجُبِ المَعْلُولِ بِإِدْرَاكِ الغَرَائِبِ المَسْبُوقِ بِمُطْلَقِ الإِدْرَاكِ المَسْبُوقِ بِالقُوَّةِ العَاقِلَةِ الَّتِي هِيَ النَّاطِقِيَّةُ، وَكَمَا أَنَّهُ لَا يُمْكِنُ تَوَهُمُ رَفْعِ النَّاطِقِ مَعَ بَقَاءِ حَقِيقَةِ الإِنْسَانِ، بِخِلَافِ الصَّاحِكِ . وَقَدْ أَشَارَ ابْنُ الحَاجِبِ فِي أَصْلِيِّهِ إِلَى الأَوَّلَيْنِ بِقَوْلِهِ : «الذَّاتِيُّ : مَا لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ فَهْمُ الذَّاتِ قَبْلَ فَهْمِهِ كَـ: اللَّوْنِيَّةِ لِلسَّوَادِ، وَالجِسْمِيَّةِ لِلإِنْسَانِ، وَقَدْ يُعَرَّفُ : بِأَنَّهُ غَيْرُ مُعَلَّلٍ وَبِالتَّرْتِيبِ العَقْلِيِّ» .
The third: that the essential is that with the assumption of its removal the essence does not remain, and the accidental is the opposite of it. Its example: rational, for it is not possible to understand the quiddity of man without it, and it is possible to understand it without 'laughing', just as it (the essential) is established for it (the essence) without a cause, while laughing is caused by wonder which is caused by the perception of marvels preceded by absolute perception preceded by the rational faculty which is rationality. And just as it is not possible to assume the removal of rational while the reality of man remains, unlike laughing. And Ibn al-Hajib in his 'Asli' referred to the first two definitions by his saying: 'The essential: that whose understanding of the essence cannot be conceived before its understanding, like color-ness for blackness and body-ness for man, and it may be defined as that which is uncaused and by intellectual order.'
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage defines the essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) using the third definition. It begins by stating that the essential is that whose removal would cause the essence not to remain; the accidental is the opposite. The example given is 'rational' (natiq) for man: one cannot conceive the quiddity of man without rationality, but one can conceive it without 'laughing' (dahim), which is an accidental property. The text then explains that rationality is uncaused for the essence, while laughing is caused by a chain: wonder, perception of marvels, absolute perception, and the rational faculty itself. This chain shows that laughing is dependent on rationality. The passage also quotes Ibn al-Hajib, who provides two earlier definitions: (1) the essential is that without which the essence cannot be understood (like color-ness for blackness), (2) it is uncaused, and (3) it is intellectually prior. This third definition is distinct from the first two, as it focuses on the assumption of removal rather than conceptual priority or causality. The student should memorize this definition and the example, as they are foundational for distinguishing essential from accidental properties in logical analysis.

Key Terms
الذاتي

Ibn al-Hajib's Two Definitions of Essential
Author cites Ibn al-Hajib's alternative definitions of essential

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and apply Ibn al-Hajib's first two definitions of the essential: conceptual priority and being uncaused, and understand the intellectual order definition.

Core Idea
Ibn al-Hajib defines essential as: (1) that without which the essence cannot be understood, (2) that which is uncaused, and (3) that which is intellectually prior.

After presenting the third definition, the author quotes Ibn al-Hajib, who summarized two earlier definitions in his work 'Asli'. These are complementary ways to identify essential properties: one based on conceptual priority, another on being uncaused, and a third based on intellectual order.

Guided Path
1
Ibn al-Hajib's first definition: conceptual priority
The essential is that without which the understanding of the essence cannot be conceived before understanding the property. Example: color-ness for blackness — you cannot understand blackness without first understanding the concept of color. Similarly, body-ness for man.
This definition focuses on the order of concepts in the mind.
2
Ibn al-Hajib's second definition: uncaused
The essential is that which is not caused by another property of the essence. It is established without a cause within the essence. Example: rationality for man is uncaused, whereas laughing has a chain of causes.
This definition looks at causality within the essence.
3
Ibn al-Hajib's additional definition: intellectual order
The essential is that which is prior in the intellect when conceiving the essence. This is similar to conceptual priority but framed as a ranking.
This is often considered equivalent to the first definition.
4
Compare with the third definition from the author
The author's third definition (assumed removal) is distinct: it tests essentiality by a thought experiment, whereas Ibn al-Hajib's definitions are based on priority and causality.
All three are valid ways to identify essential properties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثَّالِثُ : أَنَّ الذَّاتِي هُوَ الَّذِي لَا تَبْقَى الذَّاتُ مَعَ تَوَهُم رَفْعِهِ، وَالعَرَضِيَّ بِخِلَافِهِ؛ مِثَالُهُ : النَّاطِقُ ، فَإِنَّهُ لَا يُمْكِنُ فَهُمُ مَاهِيَّةِ الإِنْسَانِ بِدُونِهِ، وَيُمْكِنُ فَهْمُهَا بِدُونِ «ضَاحِكِ»، كَمَا أَنَّهُ إِذَا ثَبَتَ لَهَا بِلَا عِلَّةٍ، وَالصَّاحِكُ مَعْلُولٌ بِالتَّعَجُبِ المَعْلُولِ بِإِدْرَاكِ الغَرَائِبِ المَسْبُوقِ بِمُطْلَقِ الإِدْرَاكِ المَسْبُوقِ بِالقُوَّةِ العَاقِلَةِ الَّتِي هِيَ النَّاطِقِيَّةُ، وَكَمَا أَنَّهُ لَا يُمْكِنُ تَوَهُمُ رَفْعِ النَّاطِقِ مَعَ بَقَاءِ حَقِيقَةِ الإِنْسَانِ، بِخِلَافِ الصَّاحِكِ . وَقَدْ أَشَارَ ابْنُ الحَاجِبِ فِي أَصْلِيِّهِ إِلَى الأَوَّلَيْنِ بِقَوْلِهِ : «الذَّاتِيُّ : مَا لَا يُتَصَوَّرُ فَهْمُ الذَّاتِ قَبْلَ فَهْمِهِ كَـ: اللَّوْنِيَّةِ لِلسَّوَادِ، وَالجِسْمِيَّةِ لِلإِنْسَانِ، وَقَدْ يُعَرَّفُ : بِأَنَّهُ غَيْرُ مُعَلَّلٍ وَبِالتَّرْتِيبِ العَقْلِيِّ» .
The third: that the essential is that with the assumption of its removal the essence does not remain, and the accidental is the opposite of it. Its example: rational, for it is not possible to understand the quiddity of man without it, and it is possible to understand it without 'laughing', just as it (the essential) is established for it (the essence) without a cause, while laughing is caused by wonder which is caused by the perception of marvels preceded by absolute perception preceded by the rational faculty which is rationality. And just as it is not possible to assume the removal of rational while the reality of man remains, unlike laughing. And Ibn al-Hajib in his 'Asli' referred to the first two definitions by his saying: 'The essential: that whose understanding of the essence cannot be conceived before its understanding, like color-ness for blackness and body-ness for man, and it may be defined as that which is uncaused and by intellectual order.'
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage defines the essential (dhati) and accidental ('aradi) using the third definition. It begins by stating that the essential is that whose removal would cause the essence not to remain; the accidental is the opposite. The example given is 'rational' (natiq) for man: one cannot conceive the quiddity of man without rationality, but one can conceive it without 'laughing' (dahim), which is an accidental property. The text then explains that rationality is uncaused for the essence, while laughing is caused by a chain: wonder, perception of marvels, absolute perception, and the rational faculty itself. This chain shows that laughing is dependent on rationality. The passage also quotes Ibn al-Hajib, who provides two earlier definitions: (1) the essential is that without which the essence cannot be understood (like color-ness for blackness), (2) it is uncaused, and (3) it is intellectually prior. This third definition is distinct from the first two, as it focuses on the assumption of removal rather than conceptual priority or causality. The student should memorize this definition and the example, as they are foundational for distinguishing essential from accidental properties in logical analysis.

Key Terms
الذاتي
اللونية
الجسمية
التَّرْتِيبُ العَقْلِيُّ

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) That whose removal destroys the essence
B) That without which the essence cannot be understood before it
C) That which is caused by another property
D) That which comes after the essence in the intellect

Key Takeaway
Ibn al-Hajib defines the essential by conceptual priority, being uncaused, and intellectual order.

The Meaning of Intellectual Order (al-Tartīb al-ʿAqlī)
Author defines intellectual order and restricts it to mental existence only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the author's inclusive view of intellectual ordering (applying to both mental and external existence) from later restrictive views, and identify the logical priority of essential parts in definition.

Core Idea
Intellectual ordering means essential parts are logically prior to the whole in both mind and world.

This lesson clarifies the concept of intellectual ordering (al-tartīb al-ʿaqlī) as defined by Ibn al-Ḥājib and expanded by his commentator. The passage explains why to fully conceptualize 'human' you must first conceive animality and rationality—not just in thought but also in reality. This logical priority is the foundation of all sound definition and essential predication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the phrase 'intellectual ordering' in the Arabic. Notice it is framed as 'the meaning of intellectual ordering in Ibn al-Ḥājib's words' — this is a definition being offered.
The term is al-tartīb al-ʿaqlī.
2
Extract the domain of the ordering
Read the opening line: 'the essential of a thing precedes in both existences, mental and external.' The author explicitly states that this ordering operates in both al-wujūd al-dhihnī and al-wujūd al-khārijī.
This is a key point: some later commentators limit it to mental only.
3
Analyze the illustration
The commentator uses 'human' as an example. In the mind, you cannot conceive the quiddity (kunh) of human until you conceive its parts: animality and rationality. Externally, a human does not exist until those parts exist (as body and soul).
The parts are logically prior; not temporally prior.
4
Distinguish logical from temporal priority
The ordering is not chronological. Animality and rationality do not exist before the human; they are simply prior in the order of definition and essential constitution.
This is a common confusion.
5
Contrast with the later restrictive view
Some commentators (e.g., al-Sa'd) restrict intellectual ordering to mental existence. The author here rejects that by including external existence. Note how the passage's wording ('فِي الْوُجُودَيْنِ') insists on both.
The expansion is important for ontology and definition theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وَمَعْنَى التَّرْتِيبِ العَقْلِيِّ فِي كَلَام ابْنِ الحَاجِبِ أَنَّ ذَاتِيَّ الشَّيْءِ يَتَقَدَّمُ فِي الوُجُودَيْنِ الذَّهْنِي وَالخَارِجِيِّ) فَلَا يُتَعَقَّلُ الإِنْسَانُ فِي الذِّهْنِ أَيْ: بِالكُنْهِ حَتَّى تُتَعَقَّلَ أَجْزَاؤُهُ مِنَ الحَيَوَانِيَّةِ وَالنَّاطِقِيَّةِ، وَلَا يُوجَدُ الإِنْسَانُ فِي الخَارِجِ حَتَّى تُوجَدَ الحَيَوَانِيَّةُ وَالنَّاطِقِيَّةُ.
His statement: (And the meaning of intellectual ordering in Ibn al-Ḥājib's words is that the essential of a thing precedes in both existences, mental and external). So the human is not conceptualized in the mind—i.e., in its quiddity—until its parts from animality and rationality are conceptualized; and the human does not exist externally until animality and rationality exist.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage explains the concept of 'intellectual ordering' (al-tartīb al-ʿaqlī) as used by Ibn al-Ḥājib. The commentator begins by glossing Ibn al-Ḥājib's phrase: it means that the essential parts of a thing are prior in both mental and external existence. He then illustrates: to have a full conceptualization (taṣawwur bil-kunh) of 'human,' you must first conceive animality and rationality. Likewise, in the external world, a human does not exist unless animality and rationality (as the soul and body) exist. This priority is not temporal but logical: the parts are logically prior to the whole. The author emphasizes that this priority applies to both orders of existence, contrary to some commentators who restrict it to mental existence. The passage serves to clarify a potential ambiguity in Ibn al-Ḥājib's statement.

Key Terms
الترتيب العقلي (al-tartīb al-ʿaqlī)
الوجدان (al-wujūdān)
الكنه (al-kunh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Only mental existence
B) Only external existence
C) Both mental and external existence
D) Neither existence

Key Takeaway
Intellectual ordering is the logical priority of essential parts over the whole, applying to both mental conception and external existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition of 'human' as 'rational animal'. Explain how animality and rationality are prior in the intellectual order, both in the mind and in external reality. Specifically, why must you already understand animality to define human? And why does a human being necessarily have both animality and rationality in the external world?
?

The Five Universals

The Five Universals: Building Blocks of Definitions
Definition and commentary on the five universals with an objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five universals, explain their role in definitions, and resolve the objection about the type (sinf) being omitted.

Core Idea
The five universals are the essential tools for constructing any sound definition.

In Islamic logic, definitions are built from five universal concepts: genus, differentia, species, accident, and property. This lesson introduces these five universals and addresses a common objection regarding their completeness. By the end, you'll understand how these five categories cover all the building blocks needed for a proper definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five universals
Find the five names listed in the Arabic: جنس، فصل، نوع، عرض، خاص. Note that 'عرض' here implies 'عام' (general accident) even though it is not explicitly stated.
The author truncates 'خاص' by dropping the tā' marbūṭa.
2
Understand their role as principles of definitions
These five are called 'مبادئ التعريفات' (principles of definitions) because every complete definition (hadd) or description (rasm) is composed from these universals. Genus and differentia form a complete definition; species, accident, and property are used in descriptions.
3
Examine the objection
The text raises an objection: this division seems to exclude the 'type' (صنف) such as 'Zanjī' (black) or 'Rūmī' (Roman). These are sub-species under the species 'human'. Why are they not a separate universal?
4
Study the answer
The answer is that the type (sinf) is included under the 'non-comprehensive property' (خاصة غير شاملة). For example, 'blackness' is a property that applies only to some humans, not all. Thus it falls under 'property' (خاص). This shows that the five universals are indeed exhaustive.
The commentator points out that this answer is indicated in the question itself.
5
Connect to real definitions
Now that you know the five universals, you can analyze any Islamic definition—like شرط (condition) in usul al-fiqh—to see which universals it uses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (۳۱) وَالكُلِّيَّاتُ خَمْسَةٌ دُونَ انْتِقَاصُ : جِنْسٌ، وَفَصْلٌ، عَرَضَ، نَوْعٌ، وَخَاصٌ هَذِهِ مَبَادِئُ التَّعْرِيفَاتِ، وَهِيَ الكُلِّيَّاتُ الخَمْسُ ، وَتُسَمَّى بِاليُونَانِيَّةِ :
أورد عليه : أنَّ هذا التّقسيم غير حاصر؛ لخروج الصنف كـ الزنجي والرُّومي.
وأجيب : بأنَّ ما ذكر داخل في الخاصَّة الغير الشَّاملة، وقد أشار الشارح لهذا في السُّؤال .
ثمَّ إِنَّه كان مِن حقِّ النَّاظم أن يقول: «فالكليات» بفاء التفريع؛ لتفرع هذا عما قبله .
ورَخَّمَ «خاص» بحذف التّاء، وحَذَفَ صفة «عَرَض»؛ أي: عام.
قوله : ( بِاليُونَانِيَّةِ ) هي : لغةُ جيلٍ مِنَ النَّاس كان قبل الإسلام بكثير ، مشهورين بالعلم والحكمة؛
قيل : مِنَ الرُّوم من ولد إسحاق، وقيل: إنَّ يونان هو ابن يافث بن نوح.
His saying: (31) 'And the universals are five without deficiency: genus, differentia, accident, species, and property. These are the principles of definitions, and they are the five universals, and they are called in Greek...' An objection is raised against it: that this division is not exhaustive, because the type (sinf) like the Zanjī and the Rūmī is excluded. And it is answered: that what is mentioned is included under the non-comprehensive property (al-khāṣṣa ghayr al-shāmila), and the commentator pointed to this in the question. Then it is said: It was proper for the author to say 'fa-l-kulliyāt' with the fā' of consequence, because this branches from what preceded. And he truncated 'khāṣṣ' by dropping the tā', and he omitted the description of 'araḍ' meaning it is general. His saying: 'in Greek' is the language of a generation of people who existed long before Islam, famous for knowledge and wisdom. It is said: from the Romans, from the descendants of Isaac; and it is said: that Yūnān is the son of Yāfith son of Noah.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the five universals (al-kulliyāt al-khams) that are the building blocks of definitions. The matn line states that the universals are five, without deficiency: genus (jins), differentia (fasl), accident ('araḍ), species (naw'), and property (khāṣṣ). These are called the principles of definitions. The commentator then discusses an objection: this division is not exhaustive because it leaves out the 'type' (sinf) such as Zanjī or Rūmī (sub-species like black or white). The answer is that such types are included under the 'non-comprehensive property' (khāṣṣa ghayr shāmila), which is a specific accident that does not cover all members of the species. The commentator notes that the author should have used the particle 'fa' to indicate that this branches from the previous discussion. There is also a note on morphology: 'khāṣṣ' is truncated (by dropping the tā' marbūṭa) and the description of 'araḍ' is omitted, meaning it is general ('āmm). Finally, the term 'Greek' (yūnāniyya) is explained as the language of a people before Islam, known for knowledge, and there are two genealogies: they are either from the Romans descended from Isaac, or Yūnān is the son of Yāfith.

Key Terms
جنس
فصل
نوع
عرض
خاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how is the omission of 'type' (sinf) like Zanjī or Rūmī accounted for in the division of the five universals?
A) It is included under genus.
B) It is included under differentia.
C) It is included under non-comprehensive property.
D) It is a sixth universal.

Key Takeaway
The five universals (genus, differentia, species, accident, property) are exhaustive for definitions; the type (sinf) is a kind of property.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the concept of 'ṣalāh' (prayer). Using the five universals, identify which universal each component of its definition belongs to (e.g., 'intention' might be a property, 'standing' might be a differentia).
?
Find a definition from a usul al-fiqh text, e.g., 'al-Ḥukm is the address of the Lawgiver...' and extract the genus and differentia.

Terminology Notes: 'Khasṣ', 'Araḍ', and the Particle 'Fa'
Philological and terminological commentary on the matn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the significance of truncation in 'khāṣṣ', the omitted description of 'araḍ', and the use of 'fa' for logical branching.

Core Idea
In logical texts, every word and particle carries weight; truncation and omission are deliberate and must be understood.

The passage contains several editorial comments about the wording of the matn. The author truncated 'khāṣṣ' (dropping tā' marbūṭa), omitted the description 'general' after 'accident', and the commentator notes that a 'fa' should have been used. These are not mere pedantic points; they affect meaning. This lesson unpacks these terminological choices.

Guided Path
1
Observe the truncation of 'khāṣṣ'
The matn says 'خاص' instead of 'خاصة'. The commentator notes this is a truncation (ترخيم). In Arabic, dropping the tā' marbūṭa is permissible if the meaning remains clear. Why does it matter? 'خاص' (without tā') can also mean a special attribute, but 'خاصة' is more common for the technical term 'property'.
Despite the truncation, the context makes clear it refers to the universal 'property'.
2
Note the omitted description of 'araḍ'
The matn says 'عرض' alone, but the commentator clarifies it means 'عرض عام' (general accident). The omission is possible because the term 'accident' in this context is understood as 'general', as opposed to 'special accident' which is property.
This shows that terms can be used elliptically when the meaning is well-known.
3
Understand the missing 'fa' (فاء التفريع)
The commentator says it would have been better to say 'فالكليات' with a 'fa' to show that this follows from the previous discussion. The 'fa' of consequence (fa' al-tafrī') indicates a logical result. Without it, the connection is less explicit.
In logical texts, such particles are crucial for tracing arguments.
4
Reflect on the Greek genealogy
The passage ends with notes on the Greek people. While not directly logical, it shows awareness of the historical source of these terms. The Greek language is identified with a people known for wisdom before Islam.
This is not essential for understanding the universals but adds context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَوْلُهُ : (۳۱) وَالكُلِّيَّاتُ خَمْسَةٌ دُونَ انْتِقَاصُ : جِنْسٌ، وَفَصْلٌ، عَرَضَ، نَوْعٌ، وَخَاصٌ هَذِهِ مَبَادِئُ التَّعْرِيفَاتِ، وَهِيَ الكُلِّيَّاتُ الخَمْسُ ، وَتُسَمَّى بِاليُونَانِيَّةِ :
أورد عليه : أنَّ هذا التّقسيم غير حاصر؛ لخروج الصنف كـ الزنجي والرُّومي.
وأجيب : بأنَّ ما ذكر داخل في الخاصَّة الغير الشَّاملة، وقد أشار الشارح لهذا في السُّؤال .

These are the principles of definitions, and they are the five universals, and they are called in Greek...' And it is answered: that what is mentioned is included under the non-comprehensive property (al-khāṣṣa ghayr al-shāmila), and the commentator pointed to this in the question. This passage defines and explains the five universals (al-kulliyāt al-khams) that are the building blocks of definitions. The matn line states that the universals are five, without deficiency: genus (jins), differentia (fasl), accident ('araḍ), species (naw'), and property (khāṣṣ). These are called the principles of definitions. The commentator then discusses an objection: this division is not exhaustive because it leaves out the 'type' (sinf) such as Zanjī or Rūmī (sub-species like black or white). The answer is that such types are included under the 'non-comprehensive property' (khāṣṣa ghayr shāmila), which is a specific accident that does not cover all members of the species. The commentator notes that the author should have used the particle 'fa' to indicate that this branches from the previous discussion. There is also a note on morphology: 'khāṣṣ' is truncated (by dropping the tā' marbūṭa) and the description of 'araḍ' is omitted, meaning it is general ('āmm). Finally, the term 'Greek' (yūnāniyya) is explained as the language of a people before Islam, known for knowledge, and there are two genealogies: they are either from the Romans descended from Isaac, or Yūnān is the son of Yāfith.

Key Terms
خاص
عرض
فاء التفريع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) To emphasize the number five.
B) To show that the list of universals follows from the previous discussion.
C) To indicate a conditional statement.
D) To mark a new section.

Key Takeaway
Terminological precision, including truncation, omission, and particles, shapes the meaning of logical propositions.

The Five Universals

The Five Universals: Inside vs Outside the Essence
Author enumerates the five universals based on their relation to the essence of individuals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any universal given in an Islamic text as one of the five universals based on whether it is inside or outside the essence and whether it is general or specific.

Core Idea
The five universals are defined by their relation to the essence: inside (genus, differentia) or outside (property, general accident), with species as the complete essence.

In Islamic logic, every universal predicate falls into one of five categories. This lesson unpacks the passage that defines them by their relationship to the quiddity (māhiyya) of individuals. Once you grasp this classification, you can analyse any definition in fiqh, kalām, or uṣūl al-fiqh to see whether it is complete or defective.

Guided Path
1
Identify whether the universal is the complete essence of its individuals
If the universal gives the full reality of the individuals (like 'human' for Zayd and Amr), it is the species (naw'). Ask: does this term exhaust what it is to be each instance?
2
If not complete essence, check if it is inside the essence (a part)
Does the universal form part of the definition of the individuals? If yes, proceed to step 3. If no, it is outside the essence and you go to step 4.
3
For universals inside the essence: genus or differentia?
If the universal is more general than the species (e.g., 'animal' includes humans and horses), it is genus (jins). If it is specific to a single species (e.g., 'rational' only for humans), it is differentia (faṣl).
Note that 'rational' refers to the intellectual faculty, not speech.
4
For universals outside the essence: property or general accident?
If the universal belongs exclusively to one species (e.g., 'laughter' for humans), it is property (khāṣṣa). If it applies to multiple species (e.g., 'walker' for humans and horses), it is general accident ('araḍ 'āmm).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في مباحث الألفاظ
«إِيْسَاغُوجي» ، كَمَا يُسَمَّى بِهِ أَيْضاً العِلْمُ البَاحِثُ عَنْهَا . وَوَجْهُ انْقِسَامِ الكُلِّيِّ إِلَيْهَا :
أَنَّهُ إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ تَمَامَ مَاهِيَّةِ أَفْرَادِهِ، أَمْ) لَا :
(۱) - الأَوَّلُ : النَّوْعُ كَـ : الإِنْسَانِ»، فَإِنَّهُ تَمَامُ مَاهِيَّةِ أَفْرَادِهِ كَـ: «زَيْدٍ، وَعَمْرِو»، وَغَيْرِهِمَا ؛ لِأَنَّهَا لَمْ تَزِدْ عَلَيْهِ إِلَّا بِالعَوَارِضِ المُشخّصة.
وَالثَّاني : إِمَّا دَاخِل فِي مَاهِيَّةِ أَفْرَادِهِ بِأَنْ يَكُونَ جُزْءاً مِنْهَا ، وَإِمَّا خَارِجٌ عَنْهَا : (۲) - فَالدَّاخِلُ فِي المَاهِيَّةِ : جِنْسٌ إِنْ كَانَ أَعَمَّ مِنْهَا كَـ : «الحَيَوَانِ» الدَّاخِلِ فِي مَاهِيَّةِ الإِنْسَانِ، فَإِنَّهُ أَعَمُّ مِنْهَا لِصِدْقِهِ بِهَا ، وَبِالفَرَسِ مَثَلاً .
(۳) - وَفَصْلٌ إِنِ اخْتُصَّ بِهَا كَـ «النَّاطِقِ»، فَإِنَّهُ جُزْءٌ مِنْ مَاهِيَّةِ الإِنْسَانِ وَمُخْتَصُّ بِهَا . وَالمُرَادُ بِـ «النَّاطِقِ» : المُفَكِّرُ بِالقُوَّةِ العَاقِلَةِ ، وَلَيْسَ المُرَادُ بِهِ : المُتَكَلِّمُ بِالفِعْلِ أَوْ بِالقُوَّةِ . وَالخَارِجُ عَنِ المَاهِيَّةِ :
(٤) - إِنِ اخْتَصَّ بِحَقِيقَةٍ وَاحِدَةٍ كَـ: «الصَّاحِكِ ، وَالكَاتِبِ» لـ «الإِنْسَانِ»، فَهُوَ الخَاصَّةُ.
( (٥) - وَإِنْ شَمَلَهَا وَغَيْرَهَا كَـ: «المَاشِي بِاعْتِبَارِ الإِنْسَانِ، فَهُوَ العَرَضُ العَامُ .
-
وَقَدْ عَرَفْتَ بِمَا ذُكِرَ أَنَّ:

Chapter: On Topics of Expressions
'Isaghuji', as also called the science that investigates them. The reason for the division of the universal into them is that it is either the complete essence of its individuals, or not. 
(1) The first: species, like 'human', for it is the complete essence of its individuals like 'Zayd' and 'Amr' and others, because they add nothing to it except individuating accidents.
The second: either inside the essence of its individuals by being a part of it, or outside it. 
(2) That which is inside the essence: genus if it is more general than it, like 'animal' inside the essence of 'human', for it is more general than it because it is true of it and of, e.g., 'horse'.
(3) And differentia if it is specific to it, like 'rational', for it is a part of the essence of 'human' and specific to it. By 'rational' is meant the one who thinks with the rational faculty, not the one who speaks actually or potentially. That which is outside the essence:
(4) If it is specific to one reality, like 'laughter' and 'writer' for 'human', it is the property.
(5) And if it includes it and others, like 'walker' with respect to 'human', it is the general accident.
You have understood from what has been mentioned that species is the universal that is the complete reality, and if you wish you may say about it: 'It is what is true in answer to 'what is it?' of many agreeing in reality.'
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage defines the five universal predicables (al-kulliyyāt al-khams) by their relation to the quiddity (māhiyya) of individuals. It begins by stating the basis for division: a universal either constitutes the complete essence of its individuals, or it does not. If it is the complete essence, it is species (naw'), e.g., 'human' for Zayd and Amr. If it is not the complete essence, it is either inside the essence (as a part) or outside it. Inside the essence, if it is more general than the species, it is genus (jins), e.g., 'animal' relative to human; if it is specific to the species, it is differentia (faṣl), e.g., 'rational' for human. The passage clarifies that 'rational' means the intellectual faculty, not speech. Outside the essence, if it is specific to one species, it is property (khāṣṣa), e.g., 'laughter' or 'writer' for human; if it applies to multiple species, it is general accident ('araḍ 'āmm), e.g., 'walker' for human and other animals. The passage concludes with an alternative definition of species using the 'what is it?' formula, which will be expanded in the commentary.

Key Terms
النوع
الجنس
الفصل
الخاصة
العرض العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Differentia is more general, property is specific.
B) Differentia is inside the essence, property is outside the essence.
C) Property is a part of the essence, differentia is not.
D) Differentia is specific to one species, property applies to many.

Key Takeaway
The five universals are classified by their relation to the essence: complete (species), inside (genus and differentia), outside (property and general accident).

Definitions of Species and Genus: The 'Many' Includes Potential
Commentary explains alternative definitions of species and genus, and addresses an objection about the definition applying to one individual.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the complete definition of species and genus according to the commentators and explain how the word 'many' in the definition applies even to species with only one individual.

Core Idea
The definition of species uses 'many' to mean potential multiplicity, not actual.

This commentary refines the definitions of species and genus by adding precision: species answers 'what is it?' for many agreeing in reality, while genus answers for many differing in reality. The wording excludes accidents, differentia, and property. A key objection—species with only one individual—is resolved by interpreting 'many' as potential. This lesson unpacks these logical subtleties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Find the definitions of species and genus given in the passage. Notice that both use the 'what is it?' question and the term 'many'.
Species: 'what is true in answer to 'what is it?' of many agreeing in reality'. Genus: 'what is true in answer to 'what is it?' of many differing in reality'.
2
Understand the exclusion clauses
The phrase 'in answer to what is it?' excludes general accident (not an essence predicate); 'agreeing' in species excludes genus; 'differing' in genus distinguishes it. See how each part sharpens the definition.
The commentator also notes that 'only' should be added after 'agreeing' to exclude mixed cases.
3
Analyze the objection
The objection: species definition requires 'many', but some species (e.g., sun) have only one individual. Locate the objection and the commentator's answer.
Objection: 'species is true of many agreeing, but also true of one'.
4
Grasp the resolution
The commentator explains that 'many' includes potential individuals; the definition means 'can be true of many' not necessarily actually true of many. This preserves the definition for unique species like the sun and even non-existent species like the phoenix.
Potentiality is key: 'what can be true of many' covers both existing and non-existing individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (إِيْسَاغُوجي) هو لفظ معناه عندهم : الكلِّيَّات الخمس»، وقيل : معناه المدخل ؛ : أي: مكان الدخول في المنطق؛ سمّي بذلك باسم الحكيم الذي استخرجه ودونه.
قوله : (إِنْ كَانَ أَعَمَّ مِنْهَا ) أي : بأن كان شاملاً لها ولماهية أخرى.
قوله : (وَلَيْسَ المُرَادُ) أي : لأنَّ الأَوَّل خاصَّةٌ غير شاملة، والثَّاني شاملة.
قوله : (وَإِنْ شِئْتَ قُلْتَ فِيهِ : هُوَ : مَا صَدَقَ . .. إلخ) معنى ما صدق : ما حمل وأخبر ، وهو جنس
وَقَدْ عَرَفْتَ بِمَا ذُكِرَ أَنَّ: النَّوْعَ هُوَ : الكُلِّيُّ الَّذِي هُوَ تَمَامُ الحَقِيقَةِ، وَإِنْ شِئْتَ قُلْتَ فِيهِ: «هُوَ : مَا صَدَقَ فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ؟ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ مُتَّفِقِينَ فِي الحَقِيقَةِ»
- وَالجِنْسَ هُوَ : «جُزْءُ المَاهِيَّةِ الصَّادِقُ عَلَيْهَا وَعَلَى غَيْرِهَا»، وَإِنْ شِئْتَ قُلْتَ: «هُوَ : مَا صَدَقَ فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ؟ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ مُخْتَلِفِينَ فِي الحَقِيقَةِ».
وقوله : (فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ مخرجٌ للعَرَض العام ؛ لأنَّه لا يقع في جواب شيء، ويخرج الفصل والخاصة ؛ لأنَّهما يقعان في جواب «أيّ». وقوله : «مُتَّفِقِينَ» مخرج للجنس، فإذا سُئل عن زيد وعمرو مثلاً فقيل : «ما زيد وعمرو؟» كان: «الإنسان» جواباً عنهما ؛ لأنَّه تمام ماهيَّتهما المشتركة بينهما . ثمَّ إِنَّه كان مِن حقِّ الشَّارح أن يزيد بعد «مُتَّفِقِينَ» لفظة: «فقط»، كما فعل بعد في التنبيه الثاني؛ ليخرج الجنس الصَّادق في جواب ما هو؟» على متفقين جمعت مع مخالف لها في الحقيقة؛ كأن يُقال: «ما زيد وعمرو وخالد وهذا الفرس؟ فيقال: «الحيوان»، فقد صدق الجنس على متفقين لكن لا فقط .
فإن قيل : يَرِدُ على هذا التَّعريف أنَّ النَّوع كما يصدقُ على كثيرين متفقين، كذلك يصدق على واحد.
قلنا أجيب بأنَّ قوله : «ما صدق ... إلخ» معناه : ما صح أن يصدق؛ صدق بالفعل أم لا ؛ إذ المراد بالكثيرين ما يشمل الموجودين والمعدومين، فيدخل فيه الأنواع كلُّها ما تعددت أفراده ك: «إنسان»، وما وجد منه فرد كـ: «الشَّمس»، وما لم يوجد منه فرد كـ: «العنقاء»؛ أي: على القول بأنَّه اسم بلا مسمى، وهو الذي صدر به في القاموس»، ونصه : عنقاء مغرب طائر معروف الاسم لا الجسم، أو عظيم يبعد في طيرانه، ومعنى مغرب : أنَّها تغرب في نحو الجبل، وعلى الأوّل قول الصَّفي الحِلي :
لَمَّا رَأَيْتُ بَنِي الزَّمَانِ وَمَا بِهِم خِلٌّ وَفِيٌّ لِلشَّدَائِدِ اصْطفي أَيْقَنْتُ أَنَّ المُسْتَحِيلَ ثَلَاثَة: الغُوْلُ، وَالعَنْقَاءُ، وَالخِلُ الوَفِي
قوله : ( وَالجِنْسَ هُوَ: جُزْءُ المَاهِيَّةِ ... إلخ) مثالُهُ : ((حيوان)، فإنَّه جزء من ماهيّة «الإنسان»، وإذا سئل عن «الإنسان، والفرس» بـ «ما»، كان «الحيوان» جواباً عنهما ؛ لأنَّه تمام الماهية المشتركة بينهما .
His saying: 'Isaghuji' is a word whose meaning according to them is 'the five universals', and it is said its meaning is 'the introduction', i.e., the place of entry into logic; it is named after the sage who extracted and recorded it.
His saying: 'if it is more general than it' meaning by being inclusive of it and of another quiddity.
His saying: 'and it is not intended' meaning because the first is a property not inclusive, and the second is inclusive.
His saying: 'and if you wish you may say about it: it is what is true... etc.' The meaning of 'what is true' is what is predicated and informed, and it is a genus.
You have understood from what has been mentioned that species is the universal that is the complete reality, and if you wish you may say about it: 'It is what is true in answer to 'what is it?' of many agreeing in reality.'
And genus is 'the part of the quiddity that is true of it and of other than it', and if you wish you may say: 'It is what is true in answer to 'what is it?' of many differing in reality.'
And his saying: 'in answer to 'what is it?' excludes the general accident because it does not occur in answer to anything, and it excludes the differentia and property because they occur in answer to 'which?'. And his saying: 'agreeing' excludes genus. For if one asks about Zayd and Amr, for example, saying: 'What are Zayd and Amr?' the answer would be 'human' because it is the complete essence common between them. Then it was incumbent upon the commentator to add after 'agreeing' the word 'only', as he did later in the second notification, to exclude genus that is true in answer to 'what is it?' of agreed-upon things combined with those differing in reality; as if one says: 'What are Zayd, Amr, Khalid, and this horse?' and one responds: 'animal' — for genus is true of agreed-upon things but not only.
If it is said: There is an objection to this definition that species is true of many agreeing, likewise it is true of one.
We reply: by saying that his saying 'what is true... etc.' means what is valid to be true, actually true or not, because by 'many' is meant what includes existing and non-existing individuals. Thus it includes all species whose individuals are multiple, like 'human', those of which one individual exists, like 'sun', and those of which no individual exists, like 'anqā' (phoenix), according to the saying that it is a name without a named entity, as stated in al-Qamus: 'Anqā mughrib is a bird known in name but not in body, or a great bird that goes far in flight, and mughrib means that it disappears towards a mountain.' On the first opinion is the saying of al-Safi al-Hilli: 'When I saw the people of the time and there is no loyal friend that is chosen for hardships, I knew that the impossible is three: the ghoul, the anqā, and the loyal friend.'
His saying: 'And genus is: part of the quiddity... etc.' Its example: 'animal' is a part of the quiddity of 'human', and if one asks about 'human' and 'horse' with 'what', the answer is 'animal' because it is the complete common essence between them.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) that expands on the definitions of species and genus given in the matn. It begins by explaining the word 'Isaghuji' as meaning 'the five universals' or 'the introduction'. Then it comments on the condition for genus ('more general') by clarifying inclusive scope. It explains the alternative definition of species using the 'what is it?' formula, and then provides a similar definition for genus. The commentary carefully analyzes the wording: 'in answer to 'what is it?' excludes general accident (since it is not an essence-predicate), and also excludes differentia and property because they answer 'which?'. The word 'agreeing' in the species definition excludes genus, because genus answers 'what is it?' for things differing in reality. The commentator notes that the author should have added 'only' after 'agreeing' to handle cases where genus is predicated of a mixed group. Then an objection is raised: the definition of species requires 'many', yet species like 'sun' have only one individual. The response is that 'many' includes potential individuals; the definition means 'what can be true of many' in possibility, not necessarily in actual existence. This is illustrated with non-existent species like the phoenix. The passage ends with an example of genus: 'animal' as a part of the quiddity of both human and horse, answering 'what is it?' for both.

إِيْسَاغُوجي
ما صدق
كثيرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why does the definition of species using 'many agreeing in reality' remain valid for a species like the sun (which has only one individual)?
A) Because the sun is not truly a species
B) Because 'many' means potential multiplicity, not actual
C) Because the sun is a genus, not a species
D) Because the definition requires at least two individuals

Key Takeaway
The genus and species definitions are refined by logical conditions that ensure they apply universally, including to potential instances.

Haqiqi and Idafi Species: Definitions and Overlap
Author defines and contrasts haqiqi and idafi species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between haqiqi and idafi species and identify whether a given term is haqiqi, idafi, or both.

Core Idea
A species is real (haqiqi) when based on shared essence, and relative (idafi) when based on subsumption under a genus.

This passage builds on the earlier definition of species by splitting it into two types: haqiqi and idafi. Understanding the distinction is essential for correctly analyzing definitions and categorizations in Islamic logic, particularly when dealing with terms that can be viewed from multiple angles.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of species
The author begins by reminding us that species is a universal predicated in answer to 'what is it?' of many. Note that this is the genus of the definitions to follow.
This is the same definition from earlier lessons.
2
Extract the definitions of haqiqi and idafi
Haqiqi adds 'agreeing in reality only' to the genus; idafi adds 'subsumed under a genus'. Write down the exact Arabic phrases for each differentia.
These differentia are what distinguish the two types.
3
Analyze the relationship: generality-specificity from one aspect
The author explains that idafi is broader because it is not restricted to sharing the same reality, but narrower because it requires being under a genus. Haqiqi is the opposite. Visualize this partial overlap.
They intersect in the lowest species.
4
Identify examples of intersection and separation
The lowest species (e.g., man) is both haqiqi and idafi. The simple species (e.g., point) is only haqiqi. The lowest genus (e.g., animal) is only idafi. Match each example to the relevant category.
Man is haqiqi because all humans share essence, and idafi because it falls under 'animal' and 'body'.
5
Apply the definitions to new examples
Consider terms like 'horse' or 'body'. Determine whether they are haqiqi, idafi, or both, using the criteria from the definitions.
Check if the term applies to many sharing the same essence, and if it is under a genus.

اعْلَمْ أَنَّ النَّوْعَ قِسْمَانِ: حَقِيقِيٌّ، وَقَدْ تَقَدَّمَ أَنَّهُ: «الكُلِّيُّ المَقُولُ فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ؟ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ مُتَّفِقِينَ فِي الحَقِيقَةِ فَقَط». وَإِضَافِيٌّ، وَهُوَ: «الكُلِّيُّ المَقُولُ فِي جَوَابِ مَا هُوَ؟ عَلَى كَثِيرِينَ المُنْدَرِجِ تَحْتَ جِنْسٍ». وَمِنْ تَعْرِيفِهِمَا يُعْلَمُ أَنَّ بَيْنَهُمَا عُمُوماً وَخُصُوصاً مِنْ وَجْهِ: فَالإِضَافِيُّ أَعَمُّ مِنَ الحَقِيقِيِّ مِنْ جِهَةِ عَدَمِ تَقْيِيدِهِ بِـ: «مُتَّفِقِي الحَقِيقَةِ»، وَأَخَصُّ مِنْ جِهَةِ تَقْيِيدِهِ بِـ: المُنْدَرِجِ تَحْتَ جِنْسِ، وَالحَقِيقِيُّ بِالعَكْسِ. يَجْتَمِعَانِ فِي النَّوْعِ السَّافِلِ، وَهُوَ: الَّذِي لَا نَوْعَ تَحْتَهُ، وَيُسَمَّى: «نَوْعَ الأَنْوَاعِ» كَـ: «الإِنْسَانِ»، فَإِنَّهُ إِنَّمَا تَحْتَهُ الأَصْنَافُ وَالأَفْرَادُ، فَهُوَ حَقِيقِيٌّ؛ لِصِدْقِهِ عَلَى مُتَّفِقِي الحَقِيقَةِ فَقَط، وَإِضَافِيٌّ لِانْدِرَاجِهِ تَحْتَ جِنْسٍ، بَلْ أَجْنَاسٍ كَـ: «الحَيَوَانِ»، وَ: «الجِسْمِ». وَيَنْفَرِدُ الإِضَافِيُّ فِي الجِنْسِ السَّافِلِ: «الحَيَوَانِ»، وَالمُتَوَسِّطِ كَـ: «الجِسْمِ». وَيَنْفَرِدُ الحَقِيقِيُّ فِي النَّوْعِ البَسِيطِ؛ أَي الَّذِي لَا جِنْسَ فَوْقَهُ وَلَا نَوْعَ تَحْتَهُ كَـ: «النُّقْطَةِ»، وَ: «الوَحْدَةِ»؛ بِنَاءً عَلَى مَا لِلفَلَاسِفَةِ مِنْ كَوْنِهِمَا عَرَضَيْنِ وُجُودِيَّيْنِ، فَإِنَّهُمَا غَيْرُ مُنْدَرِجَيْنِ تَحْتَ جِنْسٍ.
Know that species is of two types: haqiqi (real), which has preceded that it is 'the universal predicated in answer to what is it? of many that agree in reality only.' And idafi (relative), which is 'the universal predicated in answer to what is it? of many that are subsumed under a genus.' From their definitions, it is known that between them is generality and specificity from one aspect: the idafi is more general than the haqiqi in terms of not being restricted by 'agreeing in reality', and more specific in terms of its restriction by 'subsumed under a genus', and the haqiqi is the opposite. They intersect in the lowest species, which is that below which there is no species, and is called 'species of species' like 'man'; for it only has genera and individuals under it, so it is haqiqi because it applies only to those agreeing in reality, and idafi because it is subsumed under a genus, rather several genera like 'animal' and 'body'. The idafi is unique to the lowest genus like 'animal' and the intermediate like 'body'. The haqiqi is unique to the simple species, meaning that above which there is no genus and below which no species, like 'point' and 'unity', based on what the philosophers hold that they are existential accidents, for they are not subsumed under a genus.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage defines two types of species: haqiqi (real) and idafi (relative). The haqiqi species is a universal that applies to many things that share the same reality (essence) — for example, 'man' applies to all individual humans, who share the same essence of rational animal. The idafi species is a universal that applies to many things that are subsumed under a genus, regardless of whether they share the same reality. The author then explains the relationship between these two: they have a partial overlap (general-specific from one aspect). The idafi species is more general than the haqiqi because it is not restricted to those sharing the same reality (e.g., 'animal' as a species relative to a higher genus is idafi, even though animals differ in essence). Conversely, the idafi is more specific because it is restricted to being under a genus, while the haqiqi can be under no genus (as in the simple species). They intersect in the lowest species, i.e., the infima species, which has no species below it, such as 'man'. Man is haqiqi because it applies only to humans who share the same essence, and idafi because it is subsumed under genera like 'animal' and 'body'. The idafi stands alone in the lowest genus (e.g., 'animal' itself, which is a genus relative to man but a species relative to 'body') and intermediate genera like 'body'. The haqiqi stands alone in the simple species, i.e., a species that has no genus above it and no species below it, according to the philosophers: examples are 'point' and 'unity'. These are considered simple existential accidents not subsumed under any genus, because if they were, they would be composite, but they are assumed to be simple.

Distinguishing Real and Relative Genus
Author defines haqiqi and idafi genus and explains their relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any universal as haqiqi genus, idafi genus, or both, by applying the conditions of identical essence and subsumption under a higher genus.

Core Idea
Real and relative genera overlap in lowest species, but diverge at the extremes of the Porphyrian tree.

In this passage, the author defines two kinds of genus: the real (haqiqi) and the relative (idafi). The real genus requires that the individuals share an identical essence, while the relative genus only requires that the universal be predicable of multiple things that fall under a higher genus. Understanding their relationship and how they combine in the lowest species is essential for correctly building Porphyrian trees, which are foundational for definition in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions
Locate the definitions of haqiqi genus (implicitly: universal predicated of things of identical essence) and idafi genus (explicitly: universal predicated of many things falling under a genus). Note the key phrases: 'identical essence' vs 'falling under a genus'.
2
Analyze the relation of generality and specificity
The author states they have 'generality and specificity from one aspect'. Map this: idafi is more general because it lacks the restriction of identical essence, but more specific because it requires falling under a higher genus. Haqiqi is the opposite. Verify with examples.
3
Examine the area of combination
The lowest species (naw' safil), e.g., 'human', is both haqiqi (true of all humans identical in essence) and idafi (falls under 'animal' and 'body'). Understand why it qualifies as both.
4
Identify exclusive territories
The idafi genus alone is found in the lowest genus (e.g., 'animal') and intermediate genera (e.g., 'body'). The haqiqi genus alone is found in simple species like 'point' and 'unity' that have no genus above them. Confirm why these examples fit.
Simple species are defined by philosophers as having no genus, hence cannot be idafi.

Section on investigations of words: with real (haqiqi) only. Genus. And relative, which is: the universal predicated in answer to 'what is it?' of many things falling under a genus. And from their definition it is known that between them is generality and specificity from one aspect: the relative is more general than the real from the angle of not being restricted by 'of identical essence', and more specific from being restricted by 'falling under a genus', and the real is the opposite. They combine in the lowest species, which is: that which has no species beneath it, and is called 'species of species', like 'human', for verily beneath it are only classes and individuals, so it is real because it is true of those identical in essence only, and relative because it falls under a genus, nay, genera like 'animal' and 'body'. And the relative is exclusive to the lowest genus, like 'animal', and the intermediate genus like 'body'. And the real is exclusive to the simple species, i.e. that which has no genus above it and no species beneath it, like 'point' and 'unity', based on the philosophers' view that they are existential accidents, for they are not subsumed under a genus, otherwise they would be composed from it and from a differentia, but they are assumed simple. His words: '(only)' he added it as a precaution against the genus true of multiple things combined with different, as previously. His words: '(and relative)' this is called relative because its being a species is by relation to what is above it in which it is subsumed. His words: '(and it is called species of species)' it is called that because it is a species of all species just as the highest genus is a genus of all genera.
Source pages p. 511

Commentary
This passage defines two types of genus: al-jins al-haqiqi (real genus) and al-jins al-idafi (relative genus). The author begins by giving the definition of the relative genus as a universal predicated in answer to 'what is it?' of multiple things falling under a genus. The real genus is implicitly defined as a universal predicated of multiple things of identical essence. Then the relationship is explained: they have generality and specificity from one aspect. The relative genus is more general than the real because it is not restricted to identical essences, but more specific because it requires falling under a higher genus. They combine in the lowest species (naw' safil), like 'human', which is both real (since it applies to all humans identical in essence) and relative (since it falls under higher genera like 'animal' and 'body'). The relative genus alone is found in the lowest genus (e.g., 'animal') and intermediate genera. The real genus alone is found in simple species like 'point' and 'unity', which according to the philosophers have no genus above them, otherwise they would be compound. Then the commentary expands on the text: 'only' is to exclude genus that applies to both identical and different essences. 'Relative' is so called because its species-nature is relative to its higher genus. 'Species of species' indicates that it is a species for all lower species, just as the highest genus is a genus for all genera. All examples are from the Islamic intellectual tradition (human, animal, body, point, unity).

Proximate and Remote Genus: The Key to Complete Definitions
Author explains the division of genus into proximate and remote for definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between proximate and remote genus and determine how each affects the completeness of a definition.

Core Idea
The distance of the genus determines whether the definition is complete or deficient.

In this lesson, we explore the poet's statement about three types of genus: proximate, remote, and intermediate. The commentator clarifies that for definitions, the relevant division is into proximate and remote. Understanding this distinction is crucial because the completeness of a definition hinges on whether the genus used is proximate or remote.

Guided Path
1
Identify the relevant division of genus
The commentator states that genus has two divisions, but only the division into proximate and remote is needed for definitions. Locate this division in the Arabic text.
2
Distinguish proximate from remote
Proximate genus is the genus closest to the species (e.g., 'animal' for 'human'). Remote genus is farther (e.g., 'body' or 'substance'). Note the example of 'animal' includes all shared aspects: growing body, absolute body, substance.
The text says: 'animal' includes all that humans and horses share.
3
Understand the definition of genus
Genus is defined as the totality of shared aspects between two quiddities. 'Totality' here means all shared predicates, not just the most specific shared part.
This is crucial to avoid confusion with 'part' (juz').
4
Apply to definition completeness
If the definition uses the proximate genus, it is complete (ḥadd tāmm). If it uses a remote genus, it is deficient (ḥadd nāqiṣ). Completeness depends on proximity, not on whether the genus is higher or lower in hierarchy.
The commentator explicitly ties completeness to proximity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ رَحِمَهُ اللهُ تَعَالَى : (۳۲) وَأَوَّلُ ثَلَاثَةٌ بِلَا شَطَط: جِنْسٌ قَرِيبٌ، أَوْ بَعِيدٌ، أَوْ وَسَط
مُرَادُهُ بـ «الأَوَّلِ» : الجِنْسُ .
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ الجِنْسَ لَهُ تَقْسِيمَانِ :
(١) - أَحَدُهُمَا - وَهُوَ الَّذِي يَجِبُ أَنْ يُحْمَلَ عَلَيْهِ كَلَامُ النَّاظِمِ؛ لِأَنَّهُ المُحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهِ غَايَةٌ في المُعَرِّفَاتِ - أَنَّهُ يَنْقَسِمُ :
۱ - إِلَى قَرِيبٍ لِلمَاهِيَّةِ ; الَّتِي هُوَ جِنْسٌ لَهَا .
۲ - وَإِلَى بَعِيدٍ عَنْهَا .
لأَنَّ الجِنْسَ قَدْ عَلِمْتَ أَنَّهُ تَمَامُ المُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَ المَاهِيَّةِ وَمَاهِيَّةٍ أُخْرَى، وَالمُرَادُ بـ «التَّمَامِ هُنَا : الجَمِيعُ، لَا الجُزْءُ المُتَمِّم ، ثُمَّ هُوَ :
قصارة.
قوله : (لِأَنَّهُ المُحْتَاجُ إِلَيْهِ) أي : لأنَّه إن كان قريباً فالتَّعريف تام، وإلا فناقص ; لأنَّ تمام الحد ونقصانَه إنَّما يترتبان على القريب والبعيد، لا على السافل والعالي .
قوله : (الجَمِيعُ) أي: جميع ما وقع فيه الاشتراك بين الماهية وماهيةٍ أخرى، وذلك كـ «الحَيَوَان»، فإنَّه شامل لجميع ما وقع فيه الاشتراك بين الإنسان والفرس مِنَ : الجسم النَّامي ، والجسم المطلق، والجوهر.
He said, may Allah have mercy on him: (32) 'And the first of three without extremeness: proximate genus, or remote, or intermediate.' By 'first' he means genus. Know that genus has two divisions: (1) One of them – and it is that upon which the words of the poet must be interpreted, because it is extremely needed in definitions – is that it divides into: 1- proximate to the quiddity for which it is a genus, 2- remote from it. Because you know that genus is the totality of the shared aspect between the quiddity and another quiddity. By 'totality' here is meant 'all', not the completing part. Then it is: (marginal note: qisara). His statement: (because it is needed) means: if it is proximate then the definition is complete, otherwise it is deficient; for the completeness and deficiency of the definition depend on the proximate and remote, not on the higher and lower. His statement: (the totality) means: all that in which the sharing occurs between the quiddity and another quiddity. For example, 'animal': it includes all that in which sharing occurs between human and horse, such as: the growing body, the absolute body, and substance.
Source pages p. 513

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the poet's line, which mentions three types of genus: proximate, remote, and intermediate. The commentator clarifies that 'the first' in the line refers to genus itself. He then explains that genus can be divided in two ways, but the relevant division for the poet's purpose is into proximate and remote. Proximate genus is the genus immediately above the species, such as 'animal' for 'human'. Remote genus is beyond that, such as 'body' or 'substance'. The commentator defines genus as the totality of the shared aspect (tamām al-mushtarak) between two quiddities, emphasizing that 'totality' here means the entire set of shared characteristics, not just the most specific shared part. He illustrates this with the example of 'animal' encompassing all that humans and horses share: growing body, absolute body, and substance. The completeness of a definition depends on whether the genus used is proximate or remote; a definition with a proximate genus is complete, while one with a remote genus is deficient. This passage thus teaches how to properly classify genus in definitions, a key tool in the five universals of logic.

القريب
البعيد
الماهية
التمام

Key Takeaway
The completeness of a definition depends on the proximity of the genus to the species; proximate genus yields a complete definition, remote genus yields a deficient one.

8 min
Close and Distant Genus
Author defines jins qarib and jins ba'id with examples from human, horse, stone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a close genus and a distant genus using the criterion of complete commonality (tamām al-mushtarak).

Core Idea
A genus is close if it fully captures what an essence shares with all partners; distant if only partial.

This passage introduces a crucial distinction within the five universals: genus can be close or distant depending on its relationship to other essences. Understanding this relativity is key to constructing accurate definitions in logic and Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the essence and its potential genus
Start with an essence like 'human'. Think of the categories it belongs to, such as 'animal', 'body', 'substance'.
The essence is the thing being defined.
2
Check for complete commonality
Determine if the alleged genus is the complete commonality (tamām al-mushtarak) between the essence and every other essence sharing that genus. For 'human', 'animal' is complete commonality with horse, cat, etc. Is there any other commonality broader? No, so it is close.
Complete commonality means no broader genus above it at that level.
3
Identify when a genus is distant
If the alleged genus is the complete commonality only with some sharers but not all, it is distant. For 'human', 'body' is complete commonality with stone but not with horse (since horse also shares 'growing' and 'sensing'). Thus 'body' is distant from 'human'.
Distant genus omits additional commonalities shared by some partners.
4
Recognize relativity
The same genus can be close to one essence and distant to another. 'Body' is close for 'stone' (all bodies share corporeality) but distant for 'human'.
Nearness and distance are relative to the essence in question.
5
Measure distance by rank
Determine how many intermediate genera exist between the distant genus and the close genus. For 'human', 'growing' is one rank distant, 'body' is two ranks, 'substance' is more.
Rank counts steps up the tree of genera.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنْ كَانَ تَمَامَ المُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَ المَاهِيَّةِ وَبَيْنَ كُلِّ مَاهِيَّةٍ تُشَارِكُهَا فِيهِ، فَهُوَ : جِنْسٌ قَرِيبٌ لَهَا كَـ: «الحَيَوَانِ» لـ «الإِنْسَانِ، فَإِنَّهُ تَمَامُ المُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَ الإِنْسَانِ وَالفَرَسِ، ثُمَّ لَا تَجِدُ مَاهِيَّةٌ تُشَارِكُ الإِنْسَانَ فِي الحَيَوَانِيَّةِ إِلَّا وَالحَيَوَانُ تَمَامُ المُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَهَا وَبَيْنَهُ، وَلَيْسَ وَرَاءَهُ تَمَامُ مُشْتَرَك آخَرُ . وَإِنْ كَانَ الجِنْسُ تَمَامَ المُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَ المَاهِيَّةِ وَبَيْنَ بَعْضِ مَا يُشَارِكُهَا دُونَ بَعْضٍ، بَعِيدٌ عَنْهَا كَـ: «الجِسْمِ بِاعْتِبَارِ مَاهِيَّةِ الإِنْسَانِ» ; لِأَنَّهُ وَإِنْ كَانَ تَمَامَ المُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَ الإِنْسَانِ وَالحَجَرِ، فَإِنَّهُ لَيْسَ تَمَامَ المُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَ الإِنْسَانِ وَالفَرَسِ مَثَلاً ; لاشْتِرَاكِهِمَا أَيْضاً فِي زَائِدٍ عَلَيْهِ كَـ : «النَّامِي»، وَ: «الحَسَّاسِ» . نَعَمْ؛ يَكُونُ الجِسْمُ قَرِيباً لِلْحَجَرِ ; لِأَنَّهُ تَمَامُ المُشْتَرَكِ بَيْنَ مَاهِيَّةِ الحَجَرِ وَبَيْنَ كُلِّ مَاهِيَّةٍ تُشَارِكُهَا فِي الجِسْمِيَّةِ، وَلَا مَحْذُورَ فِي اتِّصَافِهِ بِالقُرْبِ مِنْ شَيْءٍ وَالبُعْدِ مِنْ شَيْءٍ آخَرَ; لِأَنَّ القُرْبَ وَالْبُعْدَ نِسْيَّانِ، فَصَحَ اتِّصَافُ الجِنْسِ الوَاحِدِ بِهِمَا بِاعْتِبَارِ نِسْبَتَيْنِ مُخْتَلِفَتَيْنِ . ثُمَّ البَعِيدُ : إِمَّا بِمَرْتَبَةٍ كَـ : «النَّامِي بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِـ «الإِنْسَانِ»، أَوْ بِمَرْتَبَتَيْنِ كَـ : «الجِسْمِ) لِـ «الإِنْسَانِ) أَيْضاً، أَوْ بِأَكْثَرَ كَ: «الجَوْهَرِ» لَهُ .
If it is the complete commonality between the essence and every essence that shares with it in it, then it is a close genus for it, like 'animal' for 'human', for it is the complete commonality between human and horse, then you do not find an essence that shares with human in animality except that animal is the complete commonality between it and it, and there is no other complete commonality beyond it. And if the genus is the complete commonality between the essence and some of what shares with it but not others, it is distant from it, like 'body' with respect to the essence of human, because although it is the complete commonality between human and stone, it is not the complete commonality between human and horse, for example, because they also share in something additional to it, like 'growing' and 'sensing'. Yes, body is close for stone, because it is the complete commonality between the essence of stone and every essence that shares with it in corporeality, and there is no harm in it being described by nearness to something and distance from something else, because nearness and distance are relative, so it is correct for the one genus to be described by both with respect to two different relations. Then the distant: either by one rank, like 'growing' with respect to 'human', or by two ranks, like 'body' for 'human', or more, like 'substance' for it.
Source pages p. 514

Commentary
This passage defines the division of genus (jins) into close (qarib) and distant (ba'id). The author begins by stating the condition for a close genus: it must be the complete commonality (tamām al-mushtarak) between an essence and all other essences that share it, with no broader commonality beyond. For example, 'animal' is the close genus for 'human' because it completely captures what human shares with horse and all other animals, and no wider genus like 'body' or 'substance' does that exclusively. Conversely, a distant genus is the complete commonality between the essence and only some of its sharers. 'Body' is distant from human because while it is the complete commonality between human and stone, human and horse share additional properties (growing, sensing), so body is not the complete commonality between them. The author then uses 'body' to illustrate relativity: body is close for stone because it is the complete commonality between stone and all bodies. Nearness and distance are relative (nisbiyyān); one genus can be close to one thing and distant to another. The passage concludes by categorizing distant genera by rank: one rank (e.g., 'growing' for human), two ranks (e.g., 'body' for human), or more (e.g., 'substance' for human). This hierarchical classification is foundational for understanding definition and subdivision.

تمام المشترك
قريب
بعيد
مرتبة

Key Takeaway
A genus is close if it is the complete commonality shared with every other essence in that category; otherwise it is distant, and distance is relative and measured by ranks.

The Threefold Division of Genus: A Critical Refinement
Author critiques the poet's threefold division as contradictory to the standard twofold division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the poet's threefold division of genus, identify the author's objection that it contradicts the twofold division, and recognise the relativity of genus classifications.

Core Idea
Dividing genus into three is redundant and relative, not a logical improvement.

The poet al-nāẓim introduced a threefold division of genus: close, distant, and intermediate. While this may seem systematic, the commentator argues that it contradicts the standard twofold division and that the 'intermediate' is merely a relative label. Let's analyse the argument and see why the author insists on the simpler classification.

Guided Path
1
Recall the standard twofold division
Note that the author states the well-known division among logicians is twofold: close genus (directly above a species) and distant genus (higher in the hierarchy). This is the baseline classification.
Close genus has no lower complete commonality; distant genus has no higher complete commonality.
2
Identify the poet's threefold division
The poet adds an intermediate genus (wasaṭ) which has both a higher and lower complete commonality. For example, 'growing' for 'human' has 'animal' below and 'body' and 'substance' above.
The poet likely intended intermediate as a specific type of distant genus.
3
Analyse the author's critique
The author claims the threefold division contradicts the twofold division. He shows that what is called intermediate is actually just a distant genus with an additional property, and its classification is relative: what is intermediate for one species (e.g., 'growing' for human) is close for another (e.g., 'growing' for plant).
Relativity undermines the claim that intermediate is a distinct absolute category.
4
Evaluate the critique's validity
The critique is sound: the threefold division does not introduce a new type of genus but simply subdivides the distant genus. The relativity also shows the division is not fixed. Thus the twofold division is sufficient and more consistent.
This refinement prevents confusion in hierarchical classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَقَدْ تَبَيَّنَ : أَنَّ هَذِهِ القِسْمَةَ تُنَائِيَّةٌ ، وَذَلِكَ هُوَ المَعْرُوفُ عِنْدَهُمْ، وَالنَّاظِمُ جَعَلَهَا ثُلاثِيَّةٌ حَيْثُ قَالَ : ( جِنْسٌ قَرِيبٌ، أَوْ بَعِيدٌ، أَوْ وَسَط»، وَلَعَلَّ مُرَادَهُ بـ «الوَسَطِ» : بَعْضُ مَا يَصْدُقُ عَلَيْهِ البَعِيدُ فِي القِسْمَةِ المَذْكُورَةِ، وَذَلِكَ بِأَنْ يُحْمَلَ : البَعِيدُ عَلَى : مَا لَيْسَ فَوْقَهُ تَمَامُ مُشْتَرَك؛ كَـ : «الجَوْهَرِ» لـ «الإِنْسَانِ». وَالقَرِيبُ عَلَى : مَا لَيْسَ تَحْتَهُ تَمَامُ مُشْتَرَك؛ كَ: «الحَيَوَانِ لَهُ. وَالوَسَطُ عَلَى : مَا فَوْقَهُ تَمَامُ مُشْتَرَكَ وَتَحْتَهُ تَمَامُ مُشْتَرَك : «النَّامِي لَهُ، فَإِنَّ تَحْتَهُ كَـ الحَيَوَانُ وَفَوْقَهُ الجِسْمُ وَالجَوْهَرُ . وَهَذَا خِلَافُ المَعْرُوفِ، عَلَى أَنَّ مَا جَعَلَهُ وَسَطاً إِنَّمَا هُوَ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلإِنْسَانِ وَالفَرَسِ وَنَحْوِهِمَا ، وَهُوَ قَرِيبٌ بِالنِّسْبَةِ لِلنَّبَاتِ مَثَلاً كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ .
And it has become clear that this division is twofold, and that is the well-known view among them, but the poet made it threefold where he said: 'A close genus, or distant, or intermediate'. Perhaps he intended by 'intermediate' some of what is called distant in the mentioned division, such that: the distant is applied to what has no complete commonality above it, like 'substance' for 'human'; the close to what has no complete commonality below it, like 'animal' for him; and the intermediate to what has a complete commonality above and below it, like 'growing' for him, for below it is animal and above it is body and substance. And this is contrary to what is well-known, although what he made intermediate is only so with respect to human and horse and the like, while it is close with respect to plant, for example, as mentioned earlier.
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the poet's (al-nāẓim's) threefold division of genus. The author first confirms that the standard division among logicians is twofold (close and distant). The poet, however, introduced a third category: 'intermediate' (wasaṭ). The author suggests that the poet likely meant by 'intermediate' a certain kind of distant genus. Specifically: the distant genus is that which has no higher complete commonality above it (e.g., substance for human); the close genus has no lower complete commonality below it (e.g., animal for human); and the intermediate has both a higher and lower complete commonality (e.g., 'growing' for human: below it is animal, above it is body and substance). The author then objects that this threefold division contradicts the well-known twofold division. Moreover, the example given for 'intermediate' is only relative: 'growing' is intermediate only when considering human and horse; it is actually close relative to plant, as previously explained. This highlights the relativity of such classifications.

Key Takeaway
The classification of genus as close, distant, or intermediate is relative and the threefold division is reducible to the standard twofold division.

Division of Differentia into Close and Distant
Author draws analogy from genus to division of differentia into close and distant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between close and distant differentia in a definition and identify the close differentia for a given species.

Core Idea
Differentia, like genus, can be close or distant — close differentia sets the species apart from its immediate genus.

In the five universals, both genus and differentia have a classification into close and distant. This short passage draws the analogy explicitly, using the example of 'rational' as the close differentia for human. Understanding this division sharpens your ability to analyze definitions: when you evaluate a definition, you need to know whether the differentia given is the immediate one or a more remote one.

Guided Path
1
Recall the division of genus
The author draws an analogy to genus. Remember that genus is divided into close (genus proximum) and distant (genus remotum). For example, for 'human', the close genus is 'animal' and the distant genus is 'living being' or 'substance'.
2
Identify the close differentia in the example
The passage gives 'rational' (nāṭiq) as the close differentia for 'human'. It distinguishes human from its close genus 'animal'. So the complete definition of human is 'rational animal'.
The close differentia is always paired with the close genus.
3
Infer the concept of distant differentia
By analogy with genus, if there is a close differentia, there must be a distant differentia. The distant differentia would distinguish the species from a more distant genus. For 'human', a distant differentia might be 'thinking' or 'speaking' relative to the genus 'living being', but the passage does not give an explicit example.
The distant differentia is less specific and often not used in precise definitions.
4
Apply to another Islamic example
Consider the definition of 'fasting (sawm)' in fiqh. Its close genus is 'worship' ('ibadah). What is its close differentia? It is 'abstaining from specific things (eating, drinking, marital relations) from dawn to sunset'. That is the close differentia distinguishing it from other acts of worship like prayer.
This exercise reinforces how close genus and close differentia work together.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كَمَا يَنْقَسِمُ الجِنْسُ إِلَى : قَرِيبٍ وَبَعِيدٍ ، كَذَلِكَ الفَصْلُ أَيْضاً : يَكُونُ قَرِيباً، وَهُوَ فَصْلُ النَّوْعِ عَنْ جِنْسِهِ القَرِيبِ كَ: «النَّاطِقِ» لـ «الإِنْسَانِ».
Just as the genus divides into close and distant, similarly the differentia also: it is close, and it is the differentia of the species from its close genus, like 'rational' for 'human'.
Source pages p. 515

Commentary
This short passage extends the classification of genus to the differentia (fasl). It states that just as genus can be close or distant, the differentia also has a close type. The close differentia is the one that distinguishes the species from its close genus. For example, 'rational' (nāṭiq) is the close differentia for 'human' because it differentiates human from its close genus 'animal'. The analogy with genus implies there is also a distant differentia, though not mentioned here, which would distinguish the species from a higher, more distant genus.

Key Takeaway
